BRAZIL
MULTISTATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01         The Government of Brazil has requested the continuation of
Bank financial assistance for the expansion and improvement of water sup-
ply and sewerage systems in urban communities. The proposed project would
benefit urban communities in the states of Amazonas, Para, Paraiba, Goias,
Mato Grosso do Sul and the Federal District.   The proposed project was
prepared by the National Housing Bank (BNH) and the state water companies
(SWCs) with Bank assistance. The total cost of the project is estimated at
US$589 million equivalent, and a Bank loan of US$180 million is recommended
to finance the foreign exchange component. The Borrower would be BNH, and
the beneficiaries,  the State Water Companies (SWCs).   The works to be
financed are scheduled for construction and completion during the four-
year period 1981-84.  It is estimated that by 1984 the project would serve
about 2.5 million persons with water supply and about 0.6 million persons
would have sewerage connections.   About 63% of the additional persons
served with water in the capital cities would be urban poor.
II. THE SECTOR
Sector Development
General
2.01          Before 1971, expansion of urban infrastructure, particularly
water supply and sewerage facilities, did not keep pace with Brazil's
overall development primarily because of institutional problems and lack
of financing. Responsibility for these services was at that time vested in
the municipalities, the level of government weakest in organizational,
technical and financial capacity.  Less than 60% of the urban population
had access to public water supply and less than 30% was connected to sewage
systems.
2.02          In 1967 the Federal Government assigned responsibility for
basic sanitation to the Ministry of Interior. The National Housing Bank
(BNH) was designated as the executing agency for t'he sector and in 1971 it
launched the National Sanitation Plan (PLANASA).
2.03         The Ministry of Health's Special Public Health Service Found-
ation (FSESP) is in principle responsible for the expansion of water supply
and sewerage services in rural areas. FSESP operates through two programs
for rural areas: basic sanitation (water supply and sewerage, including
upgrading of individual sanitary units) in small communities and sanita-
tion for areas of endemic schistosomiasis. By 1978, FSESP had built 782



- 2 -
new water supply systems and extensions to 115 existing systems to benefit
a population of about 10.0 million.   It had also constructed 42 sewage
systems to benefit a 2.3 million population. There is no comprehensive
program for these areas.   FSESP systems are being transferred to the State
Water Companies (SWCs) and BNH has started to provide financial assistance
to small communities in rural areas. Regional development agencies have
also been providing sanitation assistance to the rural population through
their integrated rural development programs.  The National Department of
Sanitation Works (DNOS) has also constructed some water works.
PLANASA
2.04         PLANASA encompasses the design, financing, construction and
operation of urban water supply and sewerage services, and the training of
the sector's managerial personnel.   PLANASA is a dynamic program whose
objectives are revised periodically. Its objectives for 1985 have been
defined as follows: (a) to provide water services to 80% of the population
in 5,700 urban communities (72% out of the total of 7,900); (b) to install
or extend sewage systems in those cities with populations greater than
100,000 where individual excreta disposal systems are no longer appro-
priate; and (c) to control and decrease pollution levels in selected water-
sheds, including those near major metropolitan areas.
2.05         PLANASA is implemented through the State Water Companies
(SWCs) formed in each state by consolidating municipal water companies.
The purpose of this consolidation was to permit economies of scale, parti-
cularly in the use of qualified staff; central review of all system de-
signs; increased financial capacity, and, when appropriate, cross-subsidi-
zation and proper operation and maintenance of municipal systems. Each SWC
is responsible for construction, operation, and maintenance of the water
supply and sewage systems throughout its state. Each SWC prepared a global
feasibility study (EVG) for its state.   Revised annually, each EVG sets
forth service level objectives, construction schedules and a financing
program.      On the basis of these studies and each state's commitment to
provide one-half of the financing for the investment program from its
revolving State Water and Sewerage Fund (FAE), BNH entered into PLANASA
Agreements with all states.   These agreements incorporated the service
level objectives of the EVGs and committed BNH to providing one-half of the
financing for the investment program.  FAE funds can be used only by the
SWC and are supposed to be sufficient in the future to finance most of the
SWC investments with the objective to progressively eliminate BNH financ-
ing.   The PLANASA financing system has been successful and provided an
orderly and stable source of funds which is unusual in the sector. Financ-
ing and operating details for PLANASA are provided in Annexes 3 and 7. The
PLANASA agreements for all SWCs included in this project were renewed
during early 1981.  The PLANASA agreements for the remaining SWCs are in
the process of being renewed.



- 3-
Institutional Development
2.06          In 1974, BNH entered into the Program of Technical Assistance
for Institutional Development of State Water Companies (SATECIA) agreement
with PAHO 1/ to carry out a technical assistance program aimed at strength-
ening the execution ability and administrative efficiency of the SWCs. The
first three-year program, completed in 1977, included the analysis of each
SWC and recommendations for improvement.   A two-and-a-half year follow-up
program to assist the SWCs in implementing improvements has also been
completed.   The programs have improved the SWCs' organization and finan-
cial reporting. In November 1979, BNH introduced a technical and financial
implementation program for SWC institutional deve:Lopment (PRODISAN).   The
program will be financed by BNH under its various lines of credit. Most of
the required studies are being carried out by local consultants.   PAHO is
helping  BNH  to  supervise  the  program.    The  Brazilian  Association  of
Engineers (ABES) is providing assistance in supervising staff training.
The objective of the training program is to train annually about 20% of the
80,000 SWC employees. To date, this target has been met.
2.07         Difficulties  in recruiting qualified[  personnel  in some re-
gions, lack of managerial training of high-level staff and high turnover of
managerial staff limit the SWCs' ability to properly plan, appraise and
supervise  projects.   Thus,  the  success  of PLANASA's  implementation  is
directly linked to COSAN's 2/ staff ability to appraise and supervise SWC
operations.   The present drawbacks of the investment programs proposed by
the SWCs are:   frequent changes;  overestimation of demand for services;
overdesign of systems; underestimation of project costs; overoptimistic
execution schedules;  and excessive dependence on consultants.   Most SWCs
require the increased involvement of BNH in project preparation and super-
vision. A detailed evaluation of PLANASA is provided in Annex 3.
Service Levels
2.08          In 1980, 74% of the urban population was connected to public
water supply and 34% was served by public sewerage.3/  There are, however,
regional imbalances.   Only about 50% of the urban population in the less-
developed states has water supply service; many communities are not sup-
plied at all.   In rural areas, there are only limited sanitation facili-
ties:  less than 5% of the households have water connections.   Only 2% of
the rural households have septic tanks, 30% have Latrines, and 68% have no
satisfactory excreta disposal  facilities.   The urban poor,  however,  are
the most severely affected by the lack of sanitation services. Many of the
urban poor are recent migrants who have squatted on the periphery of
metropolitan areas where water supply does not exist, or in centrally
located but often precarious settlements.
1/ Pan American Health Organization.
2/ BNH's department in charge of the sector (para. 3.04).
3/ Based on the 1979 survey of sanitation companies by the Brazilian Asso-
ciation of Sanitary Engineers. Bank most recent social indicators indicate
that the percentage of urban population with access to safe water was 89% (in
1978) and about 84% have appropriate waste disposal.



- 4 -
2.09         PLANASA has achieved impressive results over the past decade.
The number of communities covered by the program grew from 357 in 1972 to
2,007 in 1978. The number of households connected to water supply systems
increased from 3.1 million to 8.1 million during the same period (Annex 3).
Sector Investment
2.10         During the period 1968-80 sector investments have been about
US$5 billion, with BNH providing 56% of the funds, the State Governments 33%
and grants or other contributions  11%.   PLANASA investments represented
about 0.4% of the annual GDP and have increased considerably from an average
of US$3.7 per person in urban areas in 1969-71 to US$12.8 in 1978-80, in
constant 1980 dollars (Annex 3). Investment in rural areas for 1974-78 were
US$258 million. Data on investment in urban and rural areas are in Project
File.
2.11         BINI investments are presently limited by Government efforts to
control inflation. BNH is requesting Government authorization to both in-
crease its investment program and seek additional external resources for
PLANASA. Assurances were obtained from the Government and BNH that they will
provide the funds necessary to carry out the proposed project on schedule and
that in November of each year, the Bank, BNH and the Government will analyze
the need for counterpart funds for all Bank-financed water supply and sewer-
age subprojects during the following year.
Financial Viability and Tariffs
2.12         One of the basic principles of the PLANASA program is that the
SWCs' revenues should be sufficient to ensure their financial viability.
This requirement has not been met because tariffs have not kept pace with
inflation, and because of increases in operating costs and debt service
requirements have been substantial. As a result, some SWCs have been meeting
debt service payments,  and even operating expenses,  through   loans from
BNH/FAE and state government contributions.
2.13         On May 11,  1978,  the Brazilian Congress approved   National
Tariff Law No. 6528 for the water supply and sewerage sector. The objectives
of the Law are to: (a) relate tariffs to the cost of service; (b) ensure that
water supply and sewerage services remain affordable to the low-income popu-
lation by establishing a minimum-consumption tariff related to income
level;l/ and (c) ensure the financial viability of the SWCs.  In addition,
the Law requires that tariff studies be undertaken in accordance with guide-
lines to be set by the Ministry of the Interior.
2.14         Decree No. 82587 of November 6, 1978, contains regulations to
implement the Tariff Law. The regulations set forth the procedures for and
1/  The monthly charge for the minimum consumption (about 10 m3) is not to
exceedL 35% of one readjustable National Treasury Bond (ORTN); the combined
water and sewerage charges not to exceed 50% of one ORTN.



- 5 -
frequency of tariff adjustments and define the criteria to be used in design-
ing tariff structures.   The regulations establish 1983 as the year during
which SWC revenues should be sufficient to cover the sum of operating ex-
penses, depreciation, and debt service. The maximum allowed rate of return is
equal to 12% of operating investments.l/ The 1983 objective is to be reached
through a gradual program of tariff adjustments, although interim objectives
are not specified. Additional main features of the Decree are:
(a)     SWCs will submit to BNH, by March 31 of each year, five-year
investment programs and financial plans (EVGs) which will in-
clude tariff proposals aimed at gradually attaining the fi-
nancial objectives for 1983 and thereafter.   BNH will review
these studies and submit tariff proposals to the Special Secre-
tariat for Supply and Prices (SEAP)2/ in the Ministry of Plan-
ning for approval; and
(b)     consumers are to be classified in four categories,3/ and tar-
iffs are to be progressive and allow for cross-subsidization.
2.15          Unclear definition of financial objectives in 1979-82, low ta-
riffs that are not fully adjusted for inflation, and high operating costs and
debt service have caused financial difficulties. In some states, the extreme
poverty of the population makes it difficult to generate enough revenues to
cover financial needs.   Difficulties with BENH or FAE funding and growing
investment needs cause further financial problems. The Government, BNH and
the Bank, therefore agreed in mid-1980 to review at least annually the
financial situation of each SWC receiving Bank Loans and the required tariff
increases. This agreement is being efficiently implemented (see additional
details in paras. 2.25 and 7.13 to 7.15).
Evaluation of PLANASA
2.16          Despite the efforts described in the preceding paragraphs, it
is estimated that PLANASA's initial objectives will not be achieved until
1985 for water supply and until 1990 for sewerage.4/ This may in part be the
result of excessive optimism in setting the targets, particularly in the case
of sewerage.   PLANASA's effectiveness has been affected by financial con-
straints (paras. 2.11, 2.12 and 2.15).   Unclear sector priorities at the
national level, together with political interference in the SWCs, lack of
economic analysis of projects, use of questionable techniques for least-cost
1/ Operating investments are defined as net revalued fixed assets in opera-
tion, excluding those financed by grants and including certain current assets.
2/ Prior to September 1979, authority for tariff approval was vested in the
Interministerial Price Committee (CIP), which reported to the Ministry of
Finance.
3/ Residential, commercial, industrial and public.
4/ The initial objective of PLANASA was to serve with water 80% of the urban
population by 1980 and to provide sewerage services to all main urban
centers.



- 6 -
analysis and project justification, and high per capita costs.  The Bank and
BNH agreed in previous Loans to use a selection criteria for subprojects
based on the choice of the least-cost alternative, using the incremental rate
of return as a cut-off criterion for investment in medium- and large-size
cities, and the per capita investment cost  for investments in small com-
munities. Because of insufficient follow-up by BNH, difficulties have aris-
en in the application of the criteria.  One of the major objectives of the
loan is the improvement and effective application of subproject selection
criteria to all BNH water and sewerage operations (para. 6.06).  Despite
these and other problems encountered by PLANASA (analyzed in Annex 3), the
program has proven to be basically sound and has achieved impressive results.
In addition to substantial improvements in access to safe water, PLANASA has
introduced a series of legal, financial, and administrative innovations
which will greatly facilitate the task of achieving the program's ultimate
objectives.
Environmental Legislation
2.17          In October 1973, the Federal Government created the Special
Secretariat for Environment (SEMA) in the Ministry of Interior to monitor
changes in the environment, establish air and water quality standards, and
coordinate all federal pollution control programs. Several laws and decrees
were issued thereafter to enforce pollution control.  In January 1976, the
Government issued a regulation classifying inland water resources according
to their uses, and limiting their contamination by setting up maximum permis-
sible concentrations of physical, chemical, and biological pollutants. Con-
trol of water pollution rests with SEMA, the state governments and multistate
committees.
2.18          Implementation of the pollution control laws will demand large
investments, and substantial operational and maintenance expenditures.  For
those cities needing sewage treatment, the total cost of services could be so
high that, even with substantial increases, tariffs would not be sufficient
to fully recover these costs. Capital contributions from the states and/or
the Federal Government, therefore, might be required in addition to BNH and
FAE debt financing. Sewerage tariffs will have to be structured to achieve,
where appropriate, cross-subsidization among the consumers, in particular
from the industrial ones, to make systems financially viable.   Studies to
establish appropriate tariff structures and levels for sewage collection and
treatment are expected to be completed by July 31, 1981 under the Greater Sao
Paulo Sewage Collection and Treatment Project (Loan 1525-BR).
Bank Participation in PLANASA Financing
2.19          Since 1971, the Bank has been assisting Brazil in the implemen-
tation of PLANASA through the financing of eight water supply and sewerage
projects, as shown in Annex 13.



2.20         Total actual and planned Bank assistance to the water supply
and sewerage sector is estimated to account for about 6% of the country's
sector investments in 1971-82. The only other external lenders to the sector
since 1970 have been: (a) the United States Agency for International Devel-
opment (USAID), which in 1971 extended a US$25.0 million loan to BNH for
urban sanitation; and (b) the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB), which
in 1971 provided a US$30.0 million loan to BNH for water and sewerage pro-
jects throughout Brazil.
Experience Under Previous Loans
2.21         Three of the eight Bank Loans for water supply and sewerage
(Loans 757-, 758-, and 1009-BR were completed after three-year delays. The
delays in Sao Paulo were caused by the initial failure of the State to supply
the necessary funds to complete financing, substantial technical revision of
the project, and reorganization of the executing agencies.  Service levels
exceeded expectations and the Borrower substantially improved its overall
operations.   In Minas Gerais,  the delays were attributable to the inex-
perience of the executing agency and the complexity of the project.   The
conclusions reached in the Project Performance Audit Report of April 4, 1980,
on the Sao Paulo Loans are:
(a)     the projects achieved their main objectives;
(b)     the delays caused by lack of adequate and timely state govern-
ment financing indicate the need for appropriate commitments
from the state government; and
(c)     the existence of three sector entities 1/ without a coordinat-
ing body is not conducive to efficient and effective sector
operation.
The main problems encountered during the implementation of these projects
were solved by the creation of PLANASA.   PLANASA provided the required
Government and State financing and organized the planning and implementation
of sector investments.  The creation of the SWCs to carry out the execution
and operation of works also reduced implementation problems.
2.22         Loan 1009-BR completion report stresses that the Minas Gerais
operation was the Bank's first experience in Brazil with a state-wide program
for many cities. The project was the first loan made by the Bank after the
start of PLANASA's implementation.   Initial execution delays due to poor
global feasibility studies (EVGs) and subproject approval methods resulted
in almost three years delay in project completion. The financial rates of
return were lower than covenanted due to unfulfilled assumptions that fi-
nancially viable cities would soon join COPASA and difficulties in keeping up
with high inflation rates. Nevertheless, on the overall concept the project
was successful; COPASA is a well organized company which is executing well
1/ One for water production, one for water distribution and sewage collec-
tion, and one for sewage disposal.



- 8 -
two additional loans. Furthermore, Bank participation contributed to sector
strengthening and has been the origin of a fruitful relation between BNH and
the Bank.
2.23          Because of the Government's desire to reduce inflationary ex-
pectations, it sought and obtained a waiver of the revenue covenants for
Loans 1525 and 1656-BR in early 1980. The waiver was conditioned on tariff
increases being approved in line with the Government's target for inflation
(45%).l/ Actual tariff increases for the eight SWCs being financed by the
Bank were higher and their average tariff increased almost 100% between 1979
and 1980.
2.24          The Bank and BNH have held regular discussions on improvement
of sector operations. The major operational improvements have been:
(a)     the enactment of the National Tariff Law and the development of
detailed guidelines for its implementation;
(b)     the delegation of responsibility for project approval and su-
pervision to BNH's regional offices, resulting in faster exe-
cution;
(c)     the establishment of a uniform accounting system for the SWCs,
which will enable BNH and the SWCs to have uniform and reliable
financial information; and
(d)     BNH participation in project appraisal, starting with this
project.
2.25          Further  improvements  to  be  achieved  through  this  project
include:
(a)     the establishment of sound economic principles  for economic
analysis and project selection (paras. 3.13 and 6.06);
(b)     the participation of BNH in project supervision (paras. 3.15
and 6.22);
(c)     the improvement of methodology for preparation of the EVGs
(para. 3.14);
(d)     the reinforcement and improvement of COSAN (para. 6.08);
(e)     the strengthening of the project SWCs (para. 4.16); and
(f)     the achievement of financial viability by the SWCs (para.
7.15).
2.26          These achievements would help BNH to be ready for sector
lending.

III. THE BORROWER
Organization
3.01      The Borrower for the Loan would be BNH, the Borrower for the last
five Bank Loans to Brazil in the water supply and sewerage sector.  BNH, a
tax exempt, Government-owned Bank dedicated to social development, was es-
tablished by Federal law in August 1964. It is administratively and finan-
cially independent and has reported since 1967 to the Ministry of Interior.
3.02      In 1979, BNH's assets totaled US$11 billion, and it granted loans
for about US$3 billion.   At that time, it had made 54% of its loans for
housing, 17% for urban infrastructure, 17% for water and sanitation pro-
jects, and 12% for studies, technical assistance and other activities. BNH
obtains most of its resources (70% of total liabilities) by retaining 8% of
all nongovernment salaries, which are deposited in a special Employee Indem-
nity Fund, (FGTS) 1/ that BNH administers. The contributions are maintained
in UPC 2/ and earn 3% interest p.a. Each worker may withdraw his accumulated
payroll contribution plus interest to purchase a house, or when he is unem-
ployed or retired.
3.03      BNH is administered by a Board of Directors (Conselho de Adminis-
tracao) and an Executive Committee (Diretoria). The Board consists of the
Executive Committee and nine counselors.   The Executive Committee is com-
posed of the President of the Board and seven Directors, all appointed by the
President of the country with the approval of the Federal Senate.   Each
Director heads one of the seven Operating Departments of BNH.3/   The top
management that assumed office in March 1979 is actively pursuing a program
of simplifying operations and improving efficiency. BNH operations are the
direct responsibility of the President and Department Directors, but respon-
sibility for decisions on routine matters has been delegated to Regional
Credit Committees.
BNH Sanitation Sector Operations-PLANASA Implementation
3.04      Since September 1967, BNH's Operations Division for the Sanitation
Finance System (COSAN),4/ under the Directorate of Complementary Programs
(DICOM) has been responsible for water and sewerage activites. COSAN has a
Superintendent and a Deputy Superintendent, three Advisers (Technical Analy-
sis and Programming, and Finance) (Annex 1) and 57 professional staff mem-
bers.   Of the professional staff members,  26 are financial analysts,  15
engineers  and  the  remaining  16  support  staff.    COSAN  has  overall
1/  FGTS = Fundo de Garantia por tempo de servico.
2/ The UPC (Unidade Padrao de Capital) is the value at the beginning of each
quarter of the National Treasury Securities Index ORTN.
3/ See Organization Chart in Annex 1.
4/ Carteira de Operacoes do Sistema Financeiro de Saneamento.



- 10 -
16 support staff.   COSAN has overall responsibility for expediting imple-
mentation of PLANASA by supervising the sanitation program and acting as a
technical and financial controller for the SWCs. COSAN is also responsible
for presenting tariff proposals to SEAP in the Ministry of Planning and
implementing PRODISAN (para. 2.06). COSAN also uses consultants to advise
SWCs, review documents prepared by SWCs, supervise works, study specific
topics and do research.
3.05      Some of COSAN's activities have been decentralized since 1976 to
its regional representatives in 11 states. The representatives are attached
to BNH local offices.   Regional office functions include monitoring loan
agreements between BNH and the SWCs, approving disbursements, updating pro-
ject execution and disbursement schedules, monitoring the performance of
Revolving Funds (FAEs) and Technical Agents (OTs), and reviewing SWC opera-
tions. Each regional office is responsible for at least one SWC. The need
for operational improvements in COSAN has been pointed out in previous pro-
jects, especially in the Southern States Project (Loan 1823-BR), which in-
cludes a US$200,000 technical assistance program for COSAN.  The Loan pro-
vides additional technical assistance to strengthen COSAN staff (para.
6.08). It is estimated that three engineers, three financial analysts, and
one economist should be hired as soon as possible.  Before negotiations, BNH
presented to the Bank a satisfactory implementation plan for technical as-
sistance to be provided to COSAN under Loan 1823-BR.  COSAN technical assis-
tance would be executed between June 1981 and December 1983. The employment
of consultants for this technical assistance on terms and conditions satis-
factory to the Bank is a condition of effectiveness.   Assurances were ob-
tained that BNH would approve seven additional staff positions for COSAN
before Board presentation.   The appointment of three of these seven staff
members is a condition for loan effectiveness.   The other four additional
staff for COSAN should be appointed not later than December 31, 1981.  An
evaluation of COSAN is given in Annex 3.
3.06      Most of the supervision of water and sewerage construction works
is delegated to OTs. The OTs are highly technical and respected institutions
and are under contract to BNH (para. 6.19).
3.07      Under the PLANASA agreements (para. 2.05), the parties involved
and the basic responsibilities of each entity are as follows:
(a) SWC: set priorities and prepare investment programs, operate
systems, award contracts, make payments, prepare viability
studies (EVG) by March 31 of each year and make financial and
operating reports to BNH;
(b) State Government: provide funds each year to be deposited in
the FAE to finance the sanitation works within each state
following FAE and BNH regulations, and send to BNH annual
financial statements and projections of the state income;
(c) FAE Agent (generally the State Bank): administer FAE re-
sources in accordance with BNH regulations, collect state



- 11 -
contributions and SWC debt service payments to FAE and send
BNH related financial information;
(d)  BNH Financial Agent (generally the state Bank): transfer BNH
funds to the SWC and collect debt service payments; and
(e)  BNH: in general provide regulations for the sector, in par-
ticular determine credit conditions (interest, terms) for BNH
and FAE loans, approve feasibility reports and subprojects,
supervise the SWC and subproject implementation, propose
tariff increases to the Federal Government and decide actions
to be taken if the agreements are not complied with.
3.08      The agreements established annual targets for service levels, in-
vestments, state contributions and tariffs.  These! targets are updated an-
nually through BNH approval of the EVGs.
Financing
3.09      SWC contributions to investment are very small; almost 100% of the
investment is financed by loans or grants.   In general, BNH and each FAE
finance investments in equal parts. Investments in small communities (less
than 5,000 inhabitants), however, are 70% financed by state government
grants.   The SWCs may also receive other Federal or state grants (Fundo
Perdido). Several states now require urban developers to pay for the total
cost of water and sewerage distribution networks as consumer contributions.
The total amount of grants and consumer contributions projected in the EVGs
of the project SWCs varies from 5% to 30% of total SWC investments.  Most
Federal grants for the sector are administered by BNH, but there are no clear
guidelines for distribution of the grants (para. 7.19).
3.10      The state FAEs are funded by state contributions and the interest
on funds previously lent to the SWCs. The maximum c:ontribution represents 5%
of the state revenues (mainly sales tax).  When the state contribution is
greater than 3% of its revenues, the state can obtain additional BNH loans to
make up the difference.
Subproject Preparation and Approval
3.11      Each SWC selects subprojects which are included in the EVG for BNH
consideration.  The SWC then subcontracts with consultants for the feasibi-
lity studies (Relatorio Tecnico Preliminar-RTP) which include analysis of
alternatives.  The SWC approves the RTP and sends it to the OT which then
provides its opinion and sends the RTP to BNH for approval. COSAN formally
reviews only about 5% of the RTPs presented by the SWCs. The SWC is author-
ized to contract consultants to prepare final designs (Projeto Basico) under
BNH financing. After a cycle of review and approval similar to the process
mentioned above, BNH signs a contract (CTN) with the SWC to finance the
works.



- 12 -
3.12      Each EVG consolidates the projections for many cities.  When some
SWCs have encountered problems in preparing the EVGs, COSAN staff has parti-
cipated in preparing them. All sector investment decisions have been taken
mainly on the basis of the EVGs and the projected financial position of the
SWC.
3.13      The Bank has been providing assistance to BNH to improve both the
financial and social 1/ aspects of the EVGs. The major concern has been that
the EVGs were in practice precluding the analysis of priorities and the use
of sound economic justification since decisions were made only on financial
grounds.   BNH requires the analysis of alternatives but has provided no
guidelines.   Each consultant uses different methods which do not always
ensure that  the least-cost  solution is selected.   Appropriate  selection
criteria for Bank subprojects were agreed on under previous loans, but BNH
financed several subprojects which were not found eligible for Bank financ-
ing. Assurances were obtained from BNH that not later than August 31, 1981
it will adopt the subproject selection criteria proposed for this project
(para. 6.06) to subprojects of the SWCs financed under Loans 1656-BR, 1823-BR
and 1850-BR.   This subproject selection criteria, on the basis of a study
carried out for such purpose, will be adopted for all subprojects financed by
BNH not later than December 31, 1981.
3.14      To become financially meaningful, the EVG requires several impro-
vements. The following have already been suggested by the Bank to BNH: (a)
all revenues, costs and investments should be included and projected; (b)
full annual financial statements for the period covered by the EVG should be
prepared, including the effect of inflation (estimated at 20% for 1979 and
40% for 1980 as measured in ORTN); (c) depreciation rates should be more
realistic and standarized by BNH and fixed assets (maintained in ORTN) should
be reestimated; (d) medium-term investment forecasting should be improved;
and (e) water and sewerage demand should be estimated more realistically and
a longer projection span (8-10 years) should be used.  COSAN has agreed to
implement Bank suggestions starting in the 1981 EVGs.
Bidding and Contracting
3.15      SWCs call  for bids  and awards contracts  following Federal and
state regulations. The latter vary from state to state. BNH, the OT and the
state Bank have in the past participated in the procurement process only by
formally ratifying the procedures. Some of the procedures are not compatible
with Bank procurement rules.   Bank procurement guidelines have been ex-
plained and accepted by the project SWCs.  Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that COSAN will make sure, for all project SWCs, that Bank
procurement guidelines are followed by each SWC. COSAN understands that this
will require a more active involvement in the procurement process than here-
tofore, with a corresponding reduction in the Bank's involvement.
1/ Mainly by increasing the number of poor people to be served by the SWCs.



- 13 -
3.16      Brazilian contractors often win all contracts.   Contracts are
specified in cruzeiros and subject to inflation adjustment following a poly-
nomial formula. Construction is inspected by the SWC engineering units with
some assistance from OTs. The OTs approve all bills and send them to BNH or
FAE for payment.
IV. THE BENEFICIARIES
Background
4.01      The six beneficiaries and executing agencies for the project
will be the state water companies of Amazonas (COSAMA), Para (COSANPA),
Paraiba (CAGEPA), Mato Grosso do Sul (SANESUL), Goias (SANEAGO) and the
Federal District (CAESB). These SWCs are headquartered in the state capi-
tals and are responsible for the capital city and up to several hundred
more small- or medium-size cities. The SWCs included in the project have
been participating in PLANASA, have ongoing projects financed by BNH/FAE
and have completed EVGs.
Organization and Management
4.02      All SWCs are owned by their state governmtents.  Each has a board
of directors appointed by the state governor for a period of four years.
Subsequent to changes in state governments in March 1979, new presidents
and boards of directors were appointed for most SWCs.   Except for the
president of the board of directors, who is the chief executive officer,
each director is the working head of one of the operational departments.
The president and board of directors of the project SWCs are qualified
professionals with considerable sector experience.
4.03      SWC organization,  procedures and operations  are standarized.
Organizational structures have evolved with the assistance of SATECIA
(para.  2.06) and have departments for technical operations, administra-
tion, and finance and accounting (Organization Charts, Annex 1).  These
organizational structures are considered appropriate for the activities
undertaken. An institutional analysis of each SWC is summarized in Annex
14.
4.04      The SWCs and the state governments establish priorities for the
extension of water supply and sewerage services in each state based on
assessed needs in the communities. Engineering studies, design and super-
vision of works are executed mainly through satisfactory consultants. The
SWC engineering units, with the advice and control of the OTs, review
designs and monitor construction.   Projects are usually implemented on
schedule, and project programming and control procedures are now being
strengthened to handle the increased volume of construction during 1981-
84. Technical operations are generally satisfactory. Many SWCs, however,



- 14 -
have difficulties operating distant systems, which are separated up to
2,000 km in some states.
Commercial Operations
4.05      Commercial operations are sophisticated and generally well man-
aged so as to serve a large number of municipalities scattered over a wide
geographic area, with a high percentage of people living in relative po-
verty 1/ (50% in Amazonas and Paraiba). Consumption metering is very low
(average 40%), even in some capital cities.   Lack of metering of both
production and consumption results in unreliable assessments of unac-
counted-for water (between 10-60%), and make cross-subsidization dif-
ficult. The project provides for a substantial increase of production and
consumption metering (paras. 5.10 and 5.12).
4.06      Water consumption values are distorted in some SWCs because of
the large number of unmetered connections.   These connections are billed
for 10 m3 per month in some states and up to 45 m3 per month in others. BNH
has been made aware of his problem and is implementing gradual changes to
correct these imbalances without jeopardizing the financial position of
the SWCs (para. 7.16).
4.07      Meters  are read every month and consumers  (other than those
living in remote, inaccessible communities) are billed within 15 days of
the reading. Bills are prepared by a centralized computer and collected
through local banks and SWC offices. Computer billing needs to be improved
and BNH is testing a new billing program in SANESUL to be used by all SWCs.
Collection is efficient in most SWCs (arrears are less than two months of
billing), but should be substantially improved in Goias and the Federal
District (arrears of four months of billing).   Specific targets for the
allowable percentages of accounts receivable are being proposed for each
of the project SWCs (Annex 12-Monitoring Indicators).   Fines of 10% of
billing are levied on accounts paid more than one month late, and water is
usually shut off when payment is two or more months late.
Personnel
4.08      BNH, the Federal Government, the state governments and the SWCs
have been concerned about the efficiency of SWC operations.   The six
project SWCs are making more efficient use of their personnel as shown in
Table 4.1. Training is generally satisfactory.
4.09      A major concern is the implementation of a personnel management
system that defines functions, responsibilities and salaries.2/ COSANPA has
already begun to use such a system, while the systems of the other SWCs are in
1/ Defined as families earning three minimum salaries or less.
2/ "Plano de Cargos e Salarios".



- 15 -
various stages of preparation. These systems would define technical qua-
lifications for management staff, provide incentives for technical achie-
vement and progress within the SWCs, and set forth satisfactory levels of
remuneration. The implementation of such a system would provide stability
to qualified staff and reduce the high personnel turnover that results in
frequent changes in plans and policies. Assurances were obtained from BNH
that a system of functions, responsibilities, and salaries will be imple-
mented in each project SWC not later than December 31, 1982.

Accounting-Financing
4.10      All SWCs are using the accounting system proposed by BNH in 1976.
The accounting system is satisfactory, although its reliability needs to
be improved. Accounting results are usually available within two months
after the end of each year. Full financial statements (income, balance and
cash flow) are now produced routinely.
4.11      The financial and accounting departments are headed by qualified
personnel. All project SWCs have computerized accounting systems central-
ized in their headquarters. Financial management, however, is hindered by
the lack of adequate data processing capability and the cumbersome format
of computer reports. In addition, the reports themselves are inadequate
for managerial purposes.   COSANPA owns its computer, but the other SWCs
depend on Government data processing centers for both hardware and soft-
ware.  The lack of a strong unit in charge of computer applications and
software affects the reliability of present applications and interferes
with appropriate management of all departments. Assurances were obtained
from BNH that each SWC will create a systems analysis unit which would be
responsible for all computer software not later than December 31, 1982.



- 16 -
4.12      All SWCs have prepared EVGs for 1980-84 in compliance with BNH
regulations. The EVGs presented in 1980 by the six project SWCs need to be
substantially improved both in general format (as recommended to BNH,
para. 3.14) and in practical application. During appraisal, the EVGs were
reviewed in detail with the SWCs and means of improving them were discussed
with the SWCs.
Audit
4.13      All SWCs retain external independent auditors who are generally
satisfactory. Assurances were obtained from BNH during negotiations that:
(a) accounts and financial statements for BNH and the SWCs for each fiscal
year, starting with fiscal year 1981, will be audited by independent audi-
tors satisfactory to the Bank and in accordance with generally accepted
accounting principles; (b) by April 30 for BNH and May 31 for the SWCs of
each subsequent year certified copies of the audited financial statements
and audit report will be submitted to the Bank; and (c) that the report of
the independent auditor will include a statement on compliance with the
Bank financial covenants.   BNH external auditor should also express an
opinion as to the reliability of Statements of Expenditures to support
claims for disbursements and the adequacy of associated internal control.
BNH statements of expenditures in previous loans have been satisfactory.
4.14      In addition to the external audit,  BNH continuously monitors
project disbursements. Disbursement procedures are explained in Annex 7.
When a loan contract is signed with BNH/FAE, the SWC presents a monthly
disbursement schedule with which the BNH regional office compares actual
disbursements.   When actual disbursements are 20% below the projection,
BNH/FAE disbursements are suspended until the SWC provides a satisfactory
explanation.
4.15      Some of the SWCs are using or setting up internal auditing pro-
cedures.  The complexity of the SWCs requires the prompt establishment of
internal audit offices.  Assurances were obtained from BNH that internal
audit offices will be established in the SWCs and staffed at Bank and BNH
satisfaction not later than December 31, 1982, and maintained thereafter.
Evaluation
4.16      The six SWCs  included  in the project have made considerable
progress during the last three years. They use sophisticated computer sys-
tems, and their engineering capacity is good.  They are, however, somewhat
weaker than previous beneficiaries and will require greater assistance
from BNH and the Bank.  Annex 14 summarizes the institutional strengths and
weaknesses  that should be addressed during project  supervision.   The
Bank's institution-building efforts in the SWCs will be concentrated



- 17 -
on improving three critical areas: (a) personnel (para. 4.09); (b) data
processing (para. 4.11); and (c) internal auditing (para.  4.15).  Under
this operation Bank efforts will be centered on major policy and decision
problems affecting all SWCs and the strengthening of COSAN. Less important
problems, which the Bank will not have the capacity to supervise in this
large project, should be dealt with by COSAN with Bank general assistance
(para. 6.22).
V. POPULATION, SERVICE AND DEMAND IN THE PROJECT CITIES
General
5.01      The project area, located in the center, north, and northeast of
Brazil (Map No. IBRD 15376) covers 3,867,000 km2, or 45% of the country.
The Area's 1980 population is expected to reach about 15.4 million, or
12.6% of the country's  total.   These figures are generally considered
underestimated because the project area has been the subject of recent
settlement and development programs. The 1980 Brazilian census was begun
in September 1980 and its results, to be available by mid-1981, are ex-
pected to affect the current population estimates. The population density
is 4 persons per km2, or less than one-third of the country-wide density of
14.4.  The area's climate is hot and humid.   Annex 15 contains selected
statistics for the country and each project state.
5.02      Health conditions, particularly for the poor, are inferior to
those prevailing in more developed states in the southeast and south (Annex
16).  For instance, enteritis and diarrheal diseases are frequent occur-
rences in the project area. These diseases are closely linked with unsafe
water supply and poor sanitation.
5.03      The vast project area has valuable natural resources; however,
the area produces only about 8% of the total national income. The major
economic activities are agriculture and cattle raising, which are expected
to develop rapidly in the near future. In vast areas of the states of Mato
Grosso do Sul and Goias, the potential of the soils for these activities is
very good. Development of agricultural activities in these areas is being
srongly promoted by the Government.   Summary information on the economic
resources and main development programs in the project states is in the
Project File.
Water Resources
5.04      Water resources do not limit the provision of water supply ser-
vices in the six capital cities covered by the proposed project.
1/ Sources: World Bank Tables (1980) and Brazil Human Resources Report
(1976).



- 18 -
These cities are supplied mostly from surface water sources. In addition
to surface sources, some cities use groundwater sources to supply some
isolated neighborhoods. Although the groundwater sources yield minor vol-
umes of water as compared with the surface ones,  they will have to be
maintained and expanded in the future to avoid the need for construction of
costly transmission systems.  In the city of Belem, both the surface and
groundwater systems would be needed and expanded to meet future demands.
On the contrary, in the city of Campo Grande, new surface sources are
proposed to be located 30 km from the city (para.  8.06).  In this city,
groundwater volumes of about 100 1/sec per well, could only be drawn from
600-m-deep wells drilled in basalt, with a dynamic level of about 300 m.
With the exception of Manaus and Belem, the other cities would have to
build works to regulate river flows and to ensure required volumes of water
during the dry seasons.
5.05      Water quality of all surface sources for the capital cities is
good. Conventional treatment is sufficient to produce an effluent which
meets international standards. In the city of Belem, however, the Guama
River at the intake site shows unacceptable  levels of bacteriological
contamination and high salinity requiring expensive treatment.   Belem,
with an estimated population of 900,000, has a sewerage system which serves
less than 100,000 inhabitants.   The city occupies a corner of land and
drains throughout the perimeter, including discharges in the proximity of
the water intake.   The situation will deteriorate when water supply in-
creases. Thus, measures to control sewer discharges will be studied during
project execution (para. 5.08).
5.06      The remaining medium-size cities are supplied with water from
groundwater or surface water sources. Small towns are mostly supplied from
shallow or deep-drilled wells. Groundwater quality is generally good and
requires only chlorination to meet acceptable standards.   Information on
water resources for each of the project states and capital cities is in the
Project File.
Existing Water Supply and Sewerage Systems
5.07      Water production capacity in the six capital cities covered by
the project is limited and needs expansion and/or improvement to meet
present and future demands. To reduce rationing, raw water is chlorinated
and mixed with treated water in several capital cities.   Rationing and
intermittent service due to insufficient production and inadequacy of the
distribution networks especially affects low-income neighborhoods.   The
normal capacity of many systems components has been exceeded and damages or
maintenance of one motor or pump often requires rationing.   Common needs
are: (a) expansion and improvement of intakes and treatment facilities;
(b) improvement of old water distribution systems to allow the distri-
bution of increased volumes at pressures within acceptable limits; (c)



- 19 -
extension of networks to provide services to peripheral areas; (d) in-
crease in metering coverage; and (e) improvement of operational and admin-
istrative practices of the water companies. Summary information on the
existing water supply and sewerage systems in the capital cities is given
in Annex 17. Additional information is in the Project File.
5.08       Sewage  systems  are  far  from complete  in the capital cities.
Final sewage discharges vary among the cities: (a) from part of Brasilia
into a lake, after secondary treatment, and for the remaining part of the
city, raw sewage into various creeks; (b) from Manaus and Belem screened
raw sewage, through submerged outfalls, into a river; (c) from Goiania,
Joao Pessoa and Campina Grande, raw sewage into rivers.  In Campo Grande,
the primary treatment plant, not yet in operation, lacks a sewer inter-
ceptor.   The interceptor is scheduled to be constructed next year.  The
project includes limited sewerage works in Manaus, Campo Grande, the Fed-
eral District, Joao Pessoa and Campina Grande. All capital cities urgently
need to extend their sewerage systems. Since water works are a top pri-
ority, however, and the investment capacity is limited, additional sew-
erage works will be carried out after 1983.   The master plans for waste
disposal will be prepared or updated during the execution of the project
(para. 5.11).
Population Served and Service Levels
5.09      Water supply coverage (60%) is below the PLANASA target level
for 1980 of 80% in all project states.  Only in the Federal District is
coverage at or above the average national coverage by the SWCs of 75%.
Population served by public sewerage (15%) is very low in the project
states (0-16%), other than in the Federal District, which has a sewerage
coverage of 44%.  Table 5.1 provides the mid-1980 service levels in the
project states.

- 20 -
5.10      Metered connections vary from 36-74% in the capital cities.  The
figures are much lower in the remaining cities.   The situation is even
worse if faulty meters are taken into account.  Water production is not
regularly metered. The project provides for metering of production
sources in the capital cities.   Assurances were obtained from BNH that
metering levels in all project cities with 100,000 or more inhabitants will
be at least 70% by 1982, and 80% by 1983 and thereafter.
5.11      Existing sewerage separates stormwater from domestic-industrial
sewage. The central parts of the capital cities have a drainage system.
Because of the lack of sewerage services, sewage from part of the popula-
tion is discharged into piped or open drains, polluting the environment.
The remaining population, a majority, rely on latrines or septic tanks.
Most of these individual systems are inadequate because of the population
density or because they overflow and are not properly maintained, pollut-
ing and contaminating underground sources from which part of the popula-
tion takes water. Assurances were obtained from BNH that Master Plans for
waste disposal studies will be executed in Manaus, Belem, Goiania and Campo
Grande by qualified consultants under terms of reference satisfactory to
the Bank, and that these master plans will be presented to the Bank not
later than December 31,  1983.   The studies should propose appropriate
technology solutions for areas not to be sewered, and in the case of Belem,
a comprehensive study of the coastal contamination of the Guama River in
the proximities of the water intake.
Water Production and Consumption
5.12      Lack of metering, both at the production and consumption levels,
problems with meter reading and maintenance, the large percentage assumed
as unaccounted-for water (partially due to illegal connections), distor-
tion of statistics of volumes sold to unmetered connections (especially in
Goias, Para and the Federal District) make the analysis of production and
consumption highly speculative.   Annex 18 gives statistical values for
1979 and 1980, as well as projected values for the period 1981-84. These
projections take into account the reduction of unaccounted-for water by
the implementation of metering and leak-detection programs.
Population and Demand Projections
5.13      The project area is undergoing rapid urbanization:  annual
growth indexes are 4-5% in Joao Pessoa and Belem, and 6-8% in the other
four capital cities. Specific demand studies based on available statis-
tics have been made for each capital city. Demand for additional water in
the state capitals is growing very rapidly. For the other urban communi-
ties, mostly with lower growth indexes, population projections have been
based on population estimates of the Brazilian Institute for Geography and
Statistics (IBGE). Growth rates are assumed to decline gradually. Popula-
tion, service levels, and demand statistics and projections for each state



- 21 -
are given in Annex 18.  Detailed projections for each urban community are
in the Project File.
5.14      Demand projections have been prepared for all major cities and
for the total population under each SWC. Water demand projections for each
city take into consideration: (a) the lower per capita income of the urban
poor (a large percentage of the population to be served); (b) improvement
of pressure and elimination of rationing; (c) effects of expanded metering
of house connections; and (d) demand elasticity caused by tariff increases
in real terms.   Projected growth in water sales over the period 1980-83
ranges from 6% p.a. to 26% p.a..   This compares to actual growth during
1979-80 of 8% p.a. to 21% p.a., respectively.
VI. THE PROJECT
Introduction
6.01      The project is a follow up of previous Bank operations in the
water supply and sewerage sector in Brazil. The Government requested Bank
participation in the project in 1979.  Since then, four Bank missions have
visited Brazil to assist BNH and the SWCs with project identification and
preparation.
6.02      The project will consist of the construction and extension of
water supply and sewerage systems in urban communities of the states of
AAmazonas, Goias, Mato Grosso do Sul, Para and Paraiba, and in the Federal
District. It is expected to be completed by December 31, 1984.
6.03      Bank staff appraised  subprojects  in the states of Amazonas,
Para, and Paraiba, while BNH prepared preliminary appraisal reports for
subprojects in Goias, Mato Grosso do Sul and in the Federal District. EVGs
for 1980 were only partially approved because of BNH's present financial
constraints.  BNH stopped all disbursement in September 1980 until addi-
tional budgetary allocations were obtained in November 1980. The project
states EVGs are still not satisfactory as investment tools. During project
preparation, the feasibility studies included in the investment program
for the capital cities were revised and approved after ensuring the use of
a proper methodology in the selection of the least-cost alternative for
each project component.   These subprojects were also and staged to keep
investment in line with financial resources, the executing capability of
the SWCs, and the supply of materials and equipments for the works. The
revised investment programs for each SWC are satisfactory to the Bank and
have been approved by BNH.
Project Objectives
6.04      The main objectives of the project are:



- 22-
(a)  to improve environmental and health conditions of the popu-
lation in the project area by providing safe water to an
estimated population of 2.5 million people, sewerage or
waste disposal facilities to some 0.5 million people and
pollution abatement in Brasilia; a substantial portion (62%
in the capital cities) of the incremental population served
would be urban poor (para. 8.12);
(b)  to continue Bank assistance to BNH for the improvement of
their project appraisal methods and the strengthening of
their supervision capabilities;
(c)  to improve the SWCs' management and organization, and help
strengthen their technical and financial performance; and
(d)  to assist the Government in the provision of adequate in-
frastructure to support the industrial and economic devel-
opment in the project states;
(e)  to reduce unaccounted-for water to 35% by 1983 and 30% by
1984 in all capital cities.
6.05      BNH approved investment programs for each SWC for the period
1981-84.   Annexes 4 and 5 show investments under the project.   Annex 9
gives financial projections for each SWC, including other investments dur-
ing the period 1981-84. In large cities (mostly capital cities), invest-
ments were based on detailed cost estimates of the main components of water
and sewage systems. In medium-size cities and small communities, invest-
ments are based on preliminary feasibility reports or the experience of the
SWC with other small cities.
Subproject Selection and Eligibility Criteria
6.06      Because of the large number of cities served by each SWC and the
consequent large number of subprojects subject to review, the Bank and BNH
have agreed to use a relatively simple selection criteria. These criteria
consist basically of: (a) the selection of the least-cost alternative; (b)
a cut-off criterion based on per capita investment cost for villages and
small communities; and (c) a cut-off criterion based on minimum incre-
mental rates of return for other cities. BNH is now willing to issue for
the first time guidelines for least-cost and economic analysis of subpro-
jects for all projects financed by BNH (para. 3.13 and Annex 10). The cut-
off criteria for medium and large cities will be expressed in relation to
the average long-term incremental cost (marginal cost) calculated on the
basis of the investment program of the project and its complementary works.
In cities with 50,000 or more inhabitants, the present tariff or that to be
implemented in the near future 1/ should exceed 75% of the marginal cost of
proposed water supply subprojects (equivalent to a return on the subpro-
ject of 5% or more). For water supply subprojects in cities with 5,000 to
1/ Based on covenanted tariffs in the next twelve months.



- 23 -
50,000 inhabitants or for all sewerage subprojects, the applicable tariff
(water or sewerage) should be at least higher than 60% of the marginal cost
of such subprojects (equivalent to a return of 1% or more).  The resulting
rates of return are higher than the cut-off criteria used in previous loans
to BNH in the water and sewerage sector. The use of marginal cost simpli-
fies the project selection (especially under large tariff variations with-
in the year), is easier to calculate, provides a useful insight of costs
structures, and is easier to understand for policy makers.  The marginal
cost analysis will also provide useful statistics oII per capita investment
costs, operating expenses and the cities where tariff surcharges and in-
tensive metering should be applied. Projects redesigned for minimum cost
and not satisfying the above conditions may be accepted by the Bank on
social grounds.   Acceptance of projects on social or economic grounds
requires a case by case analysis considering the percentage of urban poor
benefitted, the lack of alternative water sources, the impossibility of
recovering its costs by other than tariff charges, the health conditions of
the population and the evaluation of benefits on other sectors of the
economy.   These projects are likely to be in towIIs where urban poverty
prevails and large investments are needed to compensate lack of previous
investments.  Assurances were obtained from BNH that the proposed selec-
tion criteria (Annex 10) will be applied to all subprojects to be financed
under the loan. The Bank would later seek agreement with BNH and the other
signatories of the loan agreement to amend prior loan agreements to incor-
porate the same selection criteria for water supply and sewerage projects.
Project Description
6.07      The project consists of a group of water supply and sewerage
subprojects to be carried out in five states and the Federal District, and
of a technical assistance program for BNH and the SWqCs. A detailed project
description for each of the capital and main cities, and a tentative list
of medium and small cities to be benefited by the project are presented in
Annex 4. The population benefited from the project is analyzed in paras.
8.10 to 8.12.  The main components of the project are:
(a) construction of new water production facilities and expan-
sion or improvement of existing ones in the six capital
cities;l/
(b) construction of transmission pipel:ines to connect the new
water production facilities to the distribution system and
provision for additional storage capacity in the six capi-
tal cities;
(c)  extension of the water distribution systems to areas with-
out service and improvement of the water distribution sys-
tems to increase capacity in the six capital cities;
(d) financing of about 200,000 new house connections for water
in the six capital cites;2/
1/ Capital city includes the metropolitan areas of: each city.
2/ Other house connections would generally be financed by urban developers
or complementary investments not included in the project.



- 24 -
(e) provision of water meters for currently unmetered connec-
tions to meter at least 80% of all connections by 1984 in
the six capital cites;
(f) purchase and installation of production meters, expansion
of meter-repair shops, and implementation of leak-detection
and control programs;
(g) expansion of sewerage services in the cities of Brasilia,
Manaus, Campo Grande, Joao Pessoa, and Campina Grande;
(h) upgrading of existing sewage treatment plants in Brasilia
by adding tertiary processing (para. 6.11), and the con-
struction of sewage treatment and final disposal facilities
in other areas of the city;
(i)  financing of approximately 45,000 sewerage connections in
cities listed in (g) above;
(j) construction or expansion of water systems in approximately
80 medium-size cities;l/
(k) construction or expansion of water systems in approximately
66 small communities;
(1) provision of engineering and consulting services as re-
quired for design and construction supervision; and
(m)  provision of technical assistance programs, including con-
sulting services and equipment to BNH and the SWCs to im-
prove their operational capabilities.
6.08      Success in the implementation of PLANASA, and specifically of
the project, is directly linked to COSAN's ability to appraise and super-
vise projects and SWC operations. An implementation plan for the technical
assistance program under Loan 1823-BR was prepared by BNH before nego-
tiations of the Loan (para. 3.05). COSAN staff will be increased prior to
Loan effectiveness (para. 3.05).   The scope of the above technical as-
sistance program, however,2/ would not provide the indepth assistance
which is needed for the new functions and responsibilities being delegated
now to COSAN. The project provides funding to expand, complement and carry
out additional institution-building actions.   This additional technical
assistance has been estimated at US$1.3 million.
1/ Cities over 5,000 people excluding the capital are considered medium-
sized; those with less than 5,000 are considered as small communities.
2/ Estimated at US$200,000.



- 25 -
6.09      Although each of the project SWCs has its own specific institu-
tional and operational deficiencies, the project institutional building
and supervision effort should be concentrated on solving the following
problems:
(a) frequent changes of plans and policies and high turnover of
management staff, resulting from having political appoint-
ment of general managers, low salaries for high-level mana-
gement, and lack of a personnel manual (para. 4.09);
(b) total dependency on other institutions for all billing,
accounting and information programs (computer hardware and
software) (para. 4.11);
(c) lack of SWC internal audit offices (para. 4.15); and
(d) undervalued fixed assets and depreciation (para. 7.12).
Service Standards
6.10      Water service standards of the project are those common to other
states in Brazil, consisting of water supply by house connections and
sewage disposal by public or individual system.   Standards are in ac-
cordance with proper management practices and with the habits, expecta-
tions and ability to pay of the populations benefitted. Water production
schemes, main distribution facilities and sewerage works are appropriately
staged to suit the least-cost solutions.   Existing facilities in most
project cities will be upgraded and/or expanded in order to extend their
useful lives. Distribution networks will be extendled to new areas for the
purpose of obtaining the greatest impact on water service coverages. These
extensions are being designed to conform with accepted BNH standards.
6.11      The project includes the extension of waterborne sewage systems
in cities where the population density is too high to continue the use of
existing systems or where infiltration from alternative systems would en-
danger the quality of groundwater sources. The project includes improve-
ment of Brasilia's sewage treatment plants. Currently, sewage from down-
town Brasilia is discharged after secondary treatment into the Paranoa
Lake.   The capacity of the sewage treatment plants is very limited, and
plants are now overloaded.   Efficiency, measured in biochemical oxygen
demand removal, is about 60%.  Furthermore, the Paranoa Lake will not be
able to absorb the increasing pollution. Based on the recommendations of
prefeasibility studies prepared by consultants, CAESB attempted to sub-
stitute the existing system with a new one with final disposal in a more
distant water body (S. Bartolomeu River).  The Bank, the OT, and BNH did
not find the solution to be justified at this time.   Consequently, BNH,
CAESB and the OT selected a less costly alternative.   The alternative



- 26
includes the expansion of the sewage treatment plants and the inclusion of
tertiary treatment.   The implementation of this alternative will  also
allow lead time for further studies of the receiving bodies to be carried
out during the project construction period.
Status of Design
6.12      Consultants hired by the SWCs prepared feasibility studies (Pre-
liminary Technical Report-RTP) for the major subprojects.  The appraisal
mission reviewed the RTPs for subprojects in Manaus, Belem, Joao Pessoa,
and Campina Grande. BNH staff reviewed the subprojects in the remaining
three capital cities. These feasibility studies include works represent-
ing 78% of total project costs.  Bank recommendations on specific compo-
nents were agreed upon by BNH and the SWCs.  The proposed subprojects of
the capital cities are satisfactory to the Bank.
6.13      It is estimated that final designs corresponding to works repre-
senting about half of the total project cost are complete, and that final
designs for all major works will be completed before the end of 1981
Preparation of the final designs is not expected to delay project implemen-
tation. These final designs are being prepared by satisfactory Brazilian
consultants hired by each SWC.  RTPs and final designs for medium- and
small-size cities are being prepared by local consultants. Studies have
been prepared for most of these subprojects, but the studies need to be
updated. Their preparation has been taken into account in drawing up the
proposed implementation schedule (Annex 19).
Cost Estimates
6.14      The total cost of the project is estimated at US$589 million
equivalent, of which US$180 million (30.6%) represents the foreign ex-
change component.   The local component includes about US$20 million on
taxes to industrial products 1/. The project cost includes: (a) 10% of
the construction cost for engineering expenses related to the project; (b)
10% of the base cost for physical contingencies; and (c) US$93.9 million or
20.9% of the January 1981 project cost for price escalation.   Interest
during construction, not included in this amount, is estimated at US$49
million equivalent. Table 6.1 gives a summary of the investment costs for
each SWC. More detailed costs for major subprojects are presented in Annex
5 and summarized in Annex 6.
6.15      Cost estimates for those major subprojects in an advanced stage
of preparation are based on a bill of quantities and unit prices prepared
by consultants, the SWCs and BNH. Estimates for the other subprojects are
based on unit costs of similar works.   The costs are in January 1981
prices. Price contingencies for the local component were based on esti-
mates of the Brazilian price escalation index (measured in ORTN) of 10% in
1! 8% on pipes and meters, 5% on pumps, 8-16% on other equipments.

6.16      About 200 man-months of consultant services are included for the
technical assistance to COSAN (at about US$6,500 per man-month). Comple-
tion (Table 6.1) of final engineering designs, construction supervision
and management consultants to be hired by the SWCs will require about 6,000
man-months of consultant services at an estimated total cost of US$37
million. The average man-month cost (US$6,200), which represents the man-
month rate as billed by the consulting firm for individuals, includes:
salaries, social costs, travel, and the firm's overhead and profits. The
SWCs charge most consultant services for final engineering designs and
construction supervision to each subproject as an engineering cost.  Be-
cause of the technical ability of local consulting firms (often working in
joint ventures with foreign firms), no direct expatriate consultants' in-
put is expected with the exception of the management consultants for COSAN.
Project Financing
6.17      The SWC investment programs are to be financed by loans from BNH
and the FAEs (para. 3.09 to 3.10). The Bank Loan of US$180 million would
finance the estimated foreign exchange cost of the project, representing
30.6% of the total cost of the project. The balance would be financed by
BNH funds (19.4%) and loans from the FAEs (50%). -In addition to the normal
commitment of the Federal Government, assurances will be provided by the
State Governments to guarantee the funds necessary to carry out the pro-
posed project on schedule.
6.18      The Bank loan will be made to BNH with the guarantee of the
Government. As a condition of disbursement, each State Bank and SWC will
sign separate subsidiary loan agreements (in the form of a carta reversal
expanding the PLANASA agreement) with BNH, including all general loan
conditions and the specific covenants for each SWC.  The signing of the
first agreement satisfactory to the Bank is a condition of effectiveness.
The Loan will be allocated as follows but transfers of allocated funds
between the SWC projects would be subject to revision depending on the
actual execution schedule of each SWC:
