Project Implementation
6.19      The executing agencies for the project will be COSAMA, COSANPA,
CAGEPA, SANEAGO, SANESUL and CAESB for programs in the project states, and
BNH for the technical assistance program to COSAN.   The individual sub-
projects are being prepared and would be supervised 1/ by competent con-
sultants retained by the SWCs.   BNH would delegate overall construction
supervision and SWC assistance to the OTs. 2/ OTs are independent tech-
nical agencies contracted by BNH to review basic preparation and supervi-
sion documents.  BNH has responsibility for coordinating the entire pro-
ject, for the financial and economic evaluation, and for the technical
assistance of the SWCs.
6.20      The project will be implemented during 1981-84.  Annual project
investments are presented in Annexes 5 and 6.   These investment tables
agreed upon will form the basis for progress reporting. The construction
schedule (Annex 19) is compatible with the executing capabilities of each
SWC. The project would be completed by December 31, 1984. The closing date
would be June 30, 1985, to allow for processing pending applications.
6.21      The Bank will review and approve all subprojects in cities with
estimated investments of US$8 million or more.   The aggregate of these
subprojects would represent about 70% of the programmed investments but
only about 10% of the total number of subprojects. Subprojects in cities
in which the estimated investment is less than US$8 million would be
approved by BNH subject to ex-post Bank review on a selective basis. This
delegation of authority to BNH to approve subprojects of less than US$8
million is consistent with the institutional capabilities of BNH and in
agreement with present Bank intentions to give BNH more responsibility for
appraisal and supervision of Bank-financed projects.
Supervision
6.22      The project should strengthen BNH and consolidate Bank and BNH
work so that sector loans could become a reality.   Project objectives,
covenants and future supervision have been conceived with that purpose.
1/   Supervision of minor works is carried out by the SWC with its own
staff.
2/ The OTs for the project states are:
Amazonas and      - CETESB (Companhia de Tecnologia de Saneamento
Mato Grosso         Ambiental).
Federal District - FEEMA (Fundacao Estadual de Eng. do Meio
and Goias           Ambiente).
Para              - DNOS (Departmento Nacional de Obras de
Saneamento).
Paraiba           - SUDENE (Superintendencia Desenvolvimento do Nord-
este).



- 30 -
BNH has agreed to take an increased role in project supervision, including
procurement and subproject review which would allow to sustain present
lending levels to the sector with reasonable Bank manpower input.  Bank
traditional supervision is not feasible, and a large part of supervision
will be delegated to BNH.  To this end, COSAN personnel and organization
will be reinforced (para. 3.05). Bank supervision effort should focus on
the improvement of COSAN's management and on the major institutional prob-
lems of the SWCs (para.  6.09).  COSAN  is legally entitled to control the
operating expenses of the SWCs. Several deficiencies of the SWCs (Annex
14) should then be corrected by proper supervision of COSAN with Bank
participation,  as needed:   reduction of personnel per connection,  re-
duction of accounts receivable, control of leaks and illegal connections,
improvement of billing, statistics and demand forecasts, improvement of
the progressivity of some tariffs and elimination of billing distortions,
betterment of cost accounting and higher accuracy of intermediary annual
financial statements, etc. Expected improvements are shown in Annex 12,
"Monitoring Indicators" (para. 7.29).   Adequate Bank supervision, espe-
cially during the first two years, is considered essential for project
success. The Bank's supervision effort 1/ has been estimated at about 35
staff-weeks (SWs) for Bank fiscal year 1982 and at 28 SWs per year there-
after (para.  8.13).  Assurances were obtained from BNH that its staff will
make semiannual field supervisions of each project SWC, and will submit to
the Bank (by April 30 and October 31 of each year) a satisfactory report
on the status of project implementation, financial projections, compliance
with Bank covenants, monitoring indicators and measures taken and to be
taken to improve the SWCs' efficiency.
Procurement
6.23      Purchases of project goods and services will be processed under
contracts awarded in accordance with Bank guidelines and on the basis of
international competitive bidding. Civil works contracts under US$3 mil-
lion and contracts for equipment or materials under US$0.5 million, how-
ever, may be awarded on the basis of competitive bidding advertised locally
and in accordance with local procurement procedures which are acceptable
to the Bank.  In cases of use of local procedures, submissions from inter-
national contractors and suppliers for bidding advertised locally will not
be precluded. BNH has already made available to the Bank a general pro-
curement notice which was published on January 16, 1981 in the Development
Forum. Assurances were obtained that BNH will update the notice annually
as long as any goods or works remain to be processed.
11 Supervision requirements have been estimated as follows: one or two
visits per year to each SWC (for the first year of project implementation
and one visit per year to each SWC thereafter).   Number of missions per
year (including wrap-up with BNH), three the first year and two thereafter;
two staff members per mission.   Each field trip will be of about three
weeks. BTO report, project and procurement review to be done in head-
quarters would be three-four weeks per mission.



- 31 -
6.24      The Bank will have prior review and approval of invitation to bid
and of proposed awards for all civil works contracts estimated to cost US$3
million or more, and for all equipment contracts estimated to cost US$0.5
million or more. For all other contracts, BNH will submit to the Bank for
review copies of each contract together with the respective bidding docu-
ments, bid analysis report and recommendations for award. In evaluation of
bids for equipment and materials, local bidders will be allowed a margin of
preference of either 15% of the CIF price or the applicable import duty,
whichever is lower.   It is expected that all civil works will be won by
local contractors.  It is also expected that the bulk of the contracts for
the purchase of equipment and materials will be awarded to Brazilian firms
or Brazilian subsidiaries of foreign suppliers. Contracts for consulting
services to be financed by the Bank will be awarded in accordance with Bank
guidelines, "Uses of Consultants by the World Bank and its Borrowers -April
1974."   It is expected  that all contracts for engineering design and
supervision will be awarded to local firms.
Disbursement
6.25      The loan will be disbursed on the average as follows:
(a) 59% of BNH's disbursements to SWCs on eligible subprojects
or on SWC technical assistance programs;l/
(b) 29.5% of BNH's disbursements to the states under the FINEST
II line of credit for approved subprojects;
(c) 59% of BNH's disbursements to the states under the FINEST
III line of credit for programs in small communities;
(d) 100% of foreign expenditures and 50% of local expenditures
for consulting services for the technical assistance program
to COSAN; and
(e) 29.5% of Federal or State grants allocated to the SWCs for
water or sewerage subprojects in which at least 70% of the
population benefitted are urban poor. 2/
6.26      Agreement was obtained during negotiations that Bank disburse-
ments will be increased from 59% to 72% and from 29.5% to 36% until
December 31, 1982 or when Bank disbursements reach US$80 million, which-
ever is first, in recognition of the Government's vigorous efforts to
contain public investment expenditures.   The disbursement rate would be
adjusted thereafter so that the average disbursement rate (para.   6.25)
would be achieved.
1/  The percentage disbursed is reduced by 2.7% financial or technical
charges included in BNH disbursements.
2/ Families with an income lower than three minimum salaries.



- 32 -
6.27      In order to reduce the interval during which BNH would finance
the Bank's share of project cost with its own resources, and the underdis-
bursement of the Bank loan under high local inflation rates, BNH may
request the Bank to make advance payments from the loan account into a
Special Account, to be opened in US dollars in the Banco Central do Brasil,
and would be available for financing only the Bank's share of the project
cost.   The amount deposited in the Special Account may be increased or
decreased by the Bank as required for project execution, but should not
exceed US$12 million.  BNH would be entitled to make withdrawals from the
Special Account at the exchange rate applicable on the day the expenditures
were made. Loan disbursements will be made against a statement of expendi-
tures prepared by BNH for each subproject, the detailed documentation of
which will not be submitted for Bank review, but will be retained by the
beneficiary and made available for inspection during the course of super-
vision missions.  No works or force account are envisaged.  Disbursement
procedures are explained in Annex 7. The estimated schedule of disburse-
ments, to be completed before June 30, 1985, is shown in Annex 20.
Advance Contracting and Retroactive Financing
6.28      The SWCs included in the Multistate Project have to carry out
several high-priority works, which the SWCs and the Government believe
should be started as soon as possible.   If works are delayed until loan
signature, about one million urban poor would suffer unnecessary delays in
obtaining critically needed water and sewerage services.   BNH has re-
quested Bank authorization for advance contracting of about 15% of total
works to be bid between between February 1 and June 30, 1981, and retro-
active financing for works executed in this period.   Given the urgent
foreign exchange needs of the country and the importance to the BNH budget
of Bank reimbursements, these subprojects will have to be delayed if the
Bank does not approve BNH's request. Advance contracting for an aggregate
amount of US$90.0 million is proposed.   The works on the capital cities
proposed for advance contracting are shown in Annex 21.  Retroactive fi-
nancing is included for works in these cities executed under authorized
advance contracting up to a maximum Bank disbursement of US$7 million (3.9%
of the loan amount).
Environmental Aspects
6.29      Since the expansion of water supply systems leads to increased
volumes of sewage, it would be necessary to construct additional disposal
facilities within the next few years. This is particularly urgent in the
city of Belem where sewage discharge is causing water quality to deterio-
rate beyond permissible standards (para. 5.05). The project will help to
expand sewerage services in several large and medium cities and prepare
sewerage master plans (para. 5.11) for the capital cities of Manaus, Belem,
Goiania and Campo Grande.  The project will help to control pollution in
Paranoa Lake (Brasilia) by the expansion of secondary treatment facilities
and the construction of some tertiary processing facilities.   The con-
struction of oxidation ponds to treat sewage before discharge in other
communities of the Brasilia metropolitan area will help to reduce envi-
ronmental pollution and health hazards.



- 33 -
VII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
The Borrower
7.01       BNH is a Federal Government-owned banking institution dedicated
to development in the areas of low-cost housing, urban development, and
sanitation.  Its total assets at the end of 1979 were US$11 billion.  BNH's
primary source of funds is an 8% employee payroll contribution (Employee
Indemnity Fund, FGTS) from all nongovernment employees covered by labor
legislation (para. 3.02).   Other sources of funds include interest and
principal repayments from existing loans and investments, sale of Govern-
ment securities and deposits.
Past and Present Finances
7.02       BNH financial statements are summarized and expressed in US dol-
lars in Tables 7.1 and 7.2.  Details are given in Annex 8.  BNH's growth
during 1971-78 has been outstanding; its loan portfolio expanded 670% (40%
p.a.) and total assets grew by 570% (37% p.a.). Net receipts of the FGTS
program increased at a rate of 33% p.a. During the same period, operating
revenues grew at a rate of 34% p.a., operating expenses at 39% p.a. and net
income at 24% p.a.  BNH's annual lending reached US$3.1 billion equivalent
in 1979, which was a year of relatively slow growth in which FGTS receipts
only increased by 5%. BNH's operating expenses in 1978-79 averaged about
6% of the investment portfolio.l/
7.03       BNH requires that all its loans be guaranteed by state or muni-
cipal governments or financial institutions, or secured by mortgages. At
the end of 1979, BNH's equity was a satisfactory 13% of its total port-
folio.  BNH's major source of funds and liability is the FGTS which in-
creases by about US$1.0 billion per year. BNH's long-term debt of US$507
million equivalent was very low (15% of total assets), and debt service
represented only 1% of its net income.   BNH's creditworthiness is very
good.  The need for funds to finance housing and sanitation programs is
continuously increasing.   BNH is therefore asking the Federal Government
authorization to seek additional external funds.
7.04       Although BNH lending is very impressive (US$15 billion in the
last 9 years), BNH's rate of growth has slowed since 1975 due to the
Government policy on adjusting the ORTN. Prior to 1973, the revaluation of
the ORTN was closely related to the wholesale price index and maintained
its value regardless of inflation.   During recent years,  the ORTN has
been an instrument of monetary policy rather than an index of prices. The
increase in general prices (IGP) and the ORTN dliffered by 6.7% in 1976,
1/ The number of employees increased 15% during 1978, to 4,300. In 1980,
the number of employees is 4,500.

6.6% in 1977, 3.4% in 1978, 20.5% in 1979, and almost 40% in 1980 (Annex
9). Starting in 1981, the Government intends to maintain a close rela-
tionship between consumer price  (INPC)  increases and the ORTN.   BNH's
financial position is protected against inflation since all its lending
and liabilities are denominated in UPCs, and even short-term cash sur-
pluses are immediately converted to ORTN.
7.05       BNH's  lending terms  for the sanitation sector reflect social
rather than commercial considerations. Interest rates on BNH loans in the
sector vary from 2.0% p.a. to 7.0% p.a. The lower rates are applicable to
the poorer states (Annex 3).
BNH's Financial Projections
7.06       Each year, BNH prepares and presents  to the Ministry of the
Interior a three-year investment program expressed in UPC. Since 1980, the
Federal Government has taken direct control of and has been applying rigid
norms to the budget of decentralized Government institutions. Under these
norms, the budget for the following year should be prepared in cruzeiros at
the officially forecast inflation rate (52.4% for 1981) and presented to
the Planning Ministry (SEST) for approval. In 1980, the inflation rate,
officially forecast at 50%, reached 110%. Because of the limited budgetary
adjustment allowed by the Government, the investment programs of many
institutions have been considerably reduced. -
7.07       BNH's budget has not been as limited as those of other institu-
tions because the institution relies on its own resources. BNH investment
commitments, however, have been reduced. The reduction has caused concern
about the availability of counterpart funds for ongoing and future Bank
projects.   BNH financing for Bank projects, inc:Luding the proposed pro-
ject, would require a BNH commitment in 1981 of about US$720 million (19%
of BNH's preliminary investment budget).
7.08       BNH has shown local resources separat(ely from Bank reimburse-
ments in its budget. Thus the total BNH budget, including allocations to
COSAN and each SWC, depends now on the amount of Bank reimbursements, and
counterpart funds for the execution of Bank-financed projects have been
given the highest priority.  At the same time, the Government has estab-
lished priority budgetary allocations for projects with foreign counter-
part funds. COSAN's budget for 1981 has been increased in the same pro-
portion as the expected amount of Bank reimbursements.   In view of the
magnitude and number of Bank-financed subprojects, it is important to
review annually with BNH the amount of counterpart funds to be included in
the following year's budget (para. 2.11).
7.09       Preliminary data for 1980-82 indicates (Table 7.3) that invest-
ments on the order of US$13 billion are likely.   These would continue



- 36 -
to be financed largely by FGTS receipts and cash generated by BNH's
operations.

The Beneficiaries
7.10      The beneficiaries of this project are generally weaker than
those participating in previous Bank projects. The project SWCs are sub-
ject to large urban growth rates, expensive supplies because of the long
distance to manufacturing centers, difficulties in attracting and recruit-
ing professional staff, and lower per capita income. The project SWCs are
also more complex to manage than usual Bank project beneficiaries, since
they are responsible for one to several hundred cities scattered over wide
areas. They are relatively large companies with budgets of US$25-50 mil-
lion per year and staffs of 850 to 2,500 persons.
7.11      Considerable progress has been achieved in recent years.   The
SWCs now have planning departments which prepare the annual EVGs. Account-
ing, billing, payroll and, sometimes, inventories are computerized and
cost control measures are being implemented. Financial data, however, are
still not fully reliable, and financial statements are processed two or
three months late.  Asset revaluation, depreciation and social benefits
are frequently not adjusted until the end of the year making difficult the
interpretation of intermediary results. Under these conditions, standard



- 37 -
accounting becomes difficult to interpret meaningfully, and budgetary con-
trol is very difficult.   Wide variation in inflation forecasts makes it
preferable to present SWC financial statements in ORTN. ORTN projections
have the additional advantage that the fixed assets are by law maintained
in ORTN, and the SWC debt service payments are expr,essed in UPC.
7.12      When the SWCs were created (between 1973-79), the nominal value
of fixed assets transferred by the municipalities was recorded in books.
Incomplete fixed asset inventories and valuation, lack of correct accumu-
lated depreciation and absence of records on assets contributed by users
made the fixed assets records and estimated rates of return very inac-
curate. Assurances were be obtained from BNH that an inventory and satis-
factory valuation of fixed assets of the beneficiary SWCs will be incor-
porated in the SWCs' financial statements not later than December 31, 1982.
Revenue Covenant
7.13      Bank revenue covenants vary widely among the different projects
and are difficult to monitor  (Annex 9).   The recently enacted Tariff
Law I/provides for the sector's financial viability and its fulfillment is
being used as a target for the last two Loans 1823-BR and 1850-BR.   For
consistency within sector lending to the same Borrower and in view of
Brazilian Tariff Law, agreement will be sought in th,e near future with BNH
and the remaining signatories of prior Bank loan agreements to extend the
proposed covenant (para. 7.15) to all SWCs being financed by the Bank.
7.14      The Tariff Law requires the revenues of each SWC to be sufficient
to cover operating expenses, plus depreciation and debt service, starting
in 1983. This target is to be met progressively. Under the Brazilian Law,
the net income after depreciation (income which would produce the annual
rate of return) should be at least equal to the debt service; the maximum
annual financial rate of return on revalued fixed assets is 12%.
7.15      The Brazilian Law would normally result in excess cash since SWC
investments are 100% financed and normal working capital requirements
(about 1% of gross assets) should be lower than the depreciation rate of
fully revalued assets.   Current assets (especially accounts receivable)
are, however, not indexed, and delays in billing and collecting represent
losses in real terms. On the other hand, fixed assets are being revalued
using the ORTN index which has been increasing more slowly than inflation,
and the depreciation rate is below 2% of gross fixed assets in several
SWCs.  Thus, unless accounts receivable are properly collected and fixed
assets and depreciation realistically computed (para. 7.12), the SWCs
could end up with cash deficits rather than surpluses. The implementation
of the Brazilian Tariff Law in well managed SWCs guarantees their cash
needs and produces satisfactory rates of return.   For purposes of the
revenue covenant, it is desirable to obtain the Tariff Law objectives as
soon as possible (i.e. by 1981 rather than 1983). However, this would
1/ Law No. 6528 dated May 11, 1978 and Decree No. 82587 dated
November 6, 1978.



- 38 -
require excessively high tariff increases in real terms in some SWCs and
consequently interim financial objectives are necessary. Assurances were
obtained from the Federal Government, and BNH that the SWCs not meeting the
Tariff Law in 1981-82 will raise tariffs by at least one-third of the gap
required to comply with law in 1981, and at least two-thirds in 1982, and
that the revenues of the SWCs will satisfy the Tariff Law requirements not
later that 1983.  Satisfactory interim tariff increases for 1981 for all
SWCs being financed by the Bank were implemented before loan negotiations.
Tariff
7.16      Tariff structures for the SWCs are basically similar, with resi-
dential, commercial, industrial and public consumer categories as indi-
cated in Annex 22.  All tariff structures are progressive, with the pro-
gressivity ratio for residential consumption ranging from 1.8 to 2.5.
Tariffs for residential users are lower than for other categories.  Un-
metered consumers are charged the minimum in some SWCs while in others the
charges are based on an estimated consumption related to the physical
characteristics of the house.   The Bank has presented to BNH and the
Government several recommendations to improve the tariff structure (lower-
ing the volume at which maximum excess charges are levied, unifying the
minimum volume at 10 m3, increasing progressivity, unifying sewerage
charges as 100% of water charges, eliminating distortions between metered
and unmetered users).   These recommendations have been.accepted and are
being implemented by BNH. The tariff structures are applicable to both the
capital cities and interior municipalities. No attempts have been made to
relate charges to marginal costs.   The agreed subproject selection cri-
teria (para. 6.06) would provide statistical data on the marginal cost of
water and sewerage services in the different cities covered by each SWC.
The availability of more accurate data would allow higher charges to be
established for excess consumption in those cities with higher costs.
Average water charges are relatively high,l/ with the exception of CAESB
(the Federal District) because of past subsidies to Brasilia.   During
project execution, the high tariffs in Amazonas (COSAMA) would be reduced
in real terms. Tariffs in other states would be increased during project
execution to between US$0.10/m3 and US$0.25/m3 in September 1980 prices.
Future Finances
7.17      Financial projections for the six SWCs and the assumptions for
financial projections are presented in Annex 9. Financial statements are
presented in ORTN (para. 7.04), dividing the current value of cruzeiros by
1/ For water only the average charges in US$/m3 of September 1980 were:
COSAMA 0.30, COSANPA 0.15, CAGEPA 0.17, SANEAGO 0.13, SANESUL 0.13, CAESB
0.09. Sewerage charges generally duplicate the water tariff. These levels
of tariffs are generally higher than in other cities in Latin American
countries.

the applicable rate of the ORTN.1/ For 1981 and thereafter, the Government
intends to adjust the ORTN pari passu with the INPC. The historical dif-
ferences between the INPC and the general price index (IGP) in Brazil (8-
10% p.a.) are expected to continue, and financial projections have there-
fore been escalated at 10% for 1981, 9% for 1982 and 8% thereafter (Annex
9). Nevertheless, some expenses (unit salaries) are adjusted by law with
the INPC while others (energy price) are assumed to increase at a somewhat
faster rate than general inflation.   Financial assumptions are given in
Annex 9. A summary of selected financial data is presented in Table 7.4.
7.18      Under the agreed revenue covenant, the financial position of all
SWCs would be satisfactory. COSAMA, COSANPA and SANESUL are expected to
comply with the Tariff Law in 1981 and SANEAGO in 1982, while CAGEPA and
CAESB would fully achieve the Tariff Law targets by 1983.   In the past,
Federal and state grants, as well as BNH loans for working capital, have
provided funds needed to compensate for insufficient tariff adjustments.
States grants finance 70% of the water supply investments in small communi-
ties.   During the period of construction of the proposed project, some
additional grants have been budgeted by the state governments (especially
those of Paraiba and Mato Grosso do Sul) as well as by the Federal Dis-
trict.   Grants  (including those for small communities)  are assumed to
finance not more than 5% of investments after 1983.
7.19      The Bank has not been financing the foreign exchange component
of grant-financed sanitation works. Efficiency in the allocation of Fed-
eral and State grants distributed through BNH would be improved if these
grants are applied for poverty alleviation and to induce the SWCs to
provide services in poor areas which normally have low financial returns.
The foreign exchange component of grant-financed subprojects would be
financed by the Bank, if at least 70% of the subproject beneficiaries are
urban poor (family income below three minimum salaries).
7.20      The only risk to the financial viability of the project SWCs
after the application of appropriate subproject selection criteria would
be the implementation of indiscriminate Government price controls and
ceilings in tariff increases. Given the small effect of water cost on con-
sumer budgets and inflation indices, this risk is considered moderate.
COSAMA (Amazonas)
7.21      Amazonas is one of the poorest states in Brazil but has the
highest water tariff (US$0.30/m3).   The high tariff level is caused by
excessive personnel, high unaccounted-for water and unproductive invest-
ments.  Although major sewerage networks were installed in Manaus at the
beginning of this century, the population rebelled against sewerage tar-
iffs, and the first connections were started only at end of 1980. Future
1/ For 1979 average 387; end of year 468.7; for 1980 average 595, end of
year 706.7.



- 41 -
tariffs are expected to be lower in real terms.  COSAMA's financial posi-
tion should be satisfactory, improving its working ratio 1/ from 74% in
1980 to 55% in 1984, and reaching reasonable liquidity and a debt capi-
talization ratio of 83%. COSAMA's annual rate of return would be about 7%
on revalued  fixed assets.   During the period 1980-84,  unaccounted-for
water should be reduced from 59% to 35%, while the number of employees per
thousand water connections would decrease from 8.6 to 7.3.
COSANPA (Para)
7.22       COSANPA's water  and sewerage  service  levels are very low in
water and sewerage (63% and 5.6%, respectively). The low service levels are
the result of reduced investments caused by the lack of state counterpart
funds. Considerable investments are needed to maintain present levels of
service.   COSANPA should meet  the Tariff Law objectives and achieve a
satisfactory financial position starting in 1981. Its working ratio would
be progressively reduced from 84% in 1981 to 59% by 1984, while its capi-
talization ratio (71%) and its debt service ratio (1.6) would be practi-
cally constant  during project execution.   An annual rate of return on
revalued fixed assets of about 6.5% would be achieved  in this period.
Unaccounted-for water, now disguised by the method of estimating part of
volumes billed, will be subject to analysis and Leak detection control
(para. 6.07(f)).
CAGEPA (Paraiba)
7.23       CAGEPA was the first SWC to achieve the PLANASA goals. The large
number of small- and medium-size towns (327), the higher cost of providing
water in the Northeast and insufficient tariff adjustments created a dif-
ficult financial position in 1980, requiring state grants equivalent to
29% of the operating revenues. Even so, accounts payable are excessively
high,  reducing the current ratio to 0.5.   The pe!riod 1981-83 would be
critical. To obtain financial viability, tariff increases of about 40% in
real terms should be implemented between 1980-83..  The increases would
reduce the working ratio from 94% to 60% and increase the annual financial
rate of return on revalued fixed assets from -2% to 8.5%.   During this
period sewerage investments  for medium-size cities not included in the
project would depend on state contributions. After 1983, CAGEPA's finan-
cial position would be fully satisfactory.
SANEAGO (Goias)
7.24       Goias is a poor state with 223 cities subject to rapid urban
growth due to an intensive agricultural colonization process.   SANEAGO's
service levels are the lowest of all SWCs.   Investment during 1980 would
quadruple its fixed assets, while maintaining a capitalization ratio of
about 80%.   SANEAGO's efficiency requires improvement and the ratio of
1/ Operating cost without depreciation as a percentage of operating
revenues.



- 42 -
employees per thousand water connections should be reduced from 12.7 to 9.5
between 1980-84.   The achievement of these targets should be carefully
followed up during supervision. Distortions of the estimated water con-
sumption by unmetered connections make it impossible to determine the
level of unaccounted-for water and results in inflated tariffs.   BNH is
aware of this problem and is taking gradual measures to correct it. After
reaching the revenue level of the Tariff Law in 1982, SANEAGO's financial
position would be satisfactory with annual financial rates of return on
revalued fixed assets of about 7%, capitalization ratios of about 80%, debt
service ratios above 1.5 and decreasing working ratios (57% by 1984).
SANESUL (Mato Grosso do Sul)
7.25      SANESUL was created in 1979 after the state of Mato Grosso was
divided. SANESUL was capitalized by the State, which took over most of the
previous debt service, rescheduled some loans, and is planning to provide
additional contributions to urgently needed works.   SANESUL gross fixed
assets would increase eight times between 1980-84. SANESUL would meet the
Tariff Law target by 1981. SANESUL's financial performance should improve
rapidly as a result of the benefits of projects under execution or to be
executed and tariff increases of about 30% in real terms between 1980-84.
During this period, the annual financial rate of return on revalued fixed
assets would increase from -18% to 5.9%, and the working ratio would
decrease from 131% to 54%.  The debt service *ratio would be satisfactory
(above 1.7) while debt/capitalization would increase from 37% to 78%.
CAESB (Federal District)
7.26      As is the case with most institutions operating in the Federal
District, CAESB has depended on federal grants for operation and invest-
ment funds. The architectural design of Brasilia results in high sanita-
tion unit costs.l/ CAESB's tariffs had been the lowest in Brazil. Fur-
thermore, lack of proper administration resulted in excessive number of
employees, billing of nonexistent consumers, large uncollectable accounts
receivable and unreliable data for project planning. Substantial improve-
ments have taken place during 1980: CAESB employees have been reduced by
250 persons (10%), billings have been straightened out, accounts receiv-
able are being reduced (should reach about two months' billing by 1984),
and planning has been established, the average tariffs (US$0.09/m3) are
almost three times higher than in 1979.
7.27      Although grants  to CAESB would be phased out during project
execution, important investments in water distribution and sewerage are
still planned with contributions from the Federal Government or urban
1/ The number of meters of water or sewerage networks per person in the
"Plano Piloto" is about 4m, or almost three times the amount needed in
other cities.

Financing Plan
7.28      The financing plan for the six project SWCs during project im-
plementation (1981-84) is summarized in Table 7.6. As in all sanitation
companies in Brazil, BNH and the State Water and Sewerage Revolving Fund
(FAE) will equally finance most capital expenditures, including engineer-
ing and administrative expense and interest during construction. BNH and
FAE would finance 85.6% of the capital expenditures while federal, state
and urban developers'  contributions would finance 9.7%.   Internal cash
generation would be sufficient to cover debt service requirements, working
capital, and about 4.7% of capital expenditures. Bank reimbursements to
BNH would finance about 18.9% of the total investment and 30.6% of the
project works.
Monitoring Indicators/Reporting Requirements
7.29      As in previous loans, in order to permit the satisfactory moni-
toring of investment program and financing plans, assurances were obtained
from BNH that the SWCs will:
(a)  prepare five-year financial projections to be delivered to the
Bank before April 30 of each year, expressed in ORTN. The pro-
jections would include an income statement, statement of cash
flow, balance sheet with details on water and sewerage demand,
and programmed investments by cities;
(b)  establish, not later than December 31, 1981, a system of moni-
toring indicators and executing targets satisfactory to the Bank
(Annex 12).  Under this system, each SWC would provide a semi-
annual progress report which would include:
(i)   data on progress of project implementation, including de-
tails on design completion, physical execution of the works,
forecast project cost and disbursements tables;
(ii) statistical data on the number of water and sewer con-
nections, volume of metered water billed, total and unit
volume of water and sewerage billed, average tariffs, per-
centage of population served, number of employees, em-
ployees per 1,000 connections, percentage unaccounted-for
water;



- 45 -
(iii) financial ratios (percentage of working ratio, percentage
of accounts receivable, rate of return);
(c)  prepare a report  satisfactory to BNH and to the Bank on the
execution and initial operation of the project, not later than
three months after the loan closing date. BNH will be responsi-
ble for the preparation and submission to the Bank of the Project
Completion Report on the project within four months of loan
closing.
VIII. ECONOMIC AND SOCIAL ANALYSIS
Introduction
8.01       The economic benefits of a water supply and sewerage project are
necessarily underestimated, as many benefits are not meaningfully quanti-
fiable in monetary terms. Other benefits, (for example, enhanced property
values), cannot always be attributed to the project alone. Some well-known
benefits, such as improvement of public health, increases in effective
nutrition due to reduced internal parasites, greater well-being of the
population and effects of progressive tariffs on the distribution of in-
come and services, are simply not taken into consideriation. Therefore, the
rates of return are not comparable with those obtained in some other
sectors. The project supports Government efforts to achieve more balanced
urban development and will provide sorely needed water and sewerage ser-
vices to some of the most deprived regions in Brazil.
8.02       Project preparation and execution should contribute greatly to
the strengthening of BNH and the executing agencies  (SWCs).   The most
important aspect of this is BNH's agreement to issue sound economic guide-
lines for least-cost analysis and subproject select-ion. These guidelines
(para. 6.06) will be applied to all projects financed by BNH.
Least-Cost Analysis
8.03       Least-cost  solutions  for all  state capitals  and the city of
Campina Grande (Paraiba) were analyzed in detail.   As a result of these
analyses, investments for the first stages of water supply subprojects in
Belem, Joao Pessoa, and Manaus, and the sewage treatment in Brasilia were
greatly reduced.   Appropriate methods  for least-cost analysis will be
incorporated in all feasibility reports presented to BNH (para. 6.06).
Rates of Return and Marginal Cost
8.04       A detailed economic analysis was made of each of the state capi-
tals (details in Annex 11).  A similar analysis was made of the overall

investment program in each SWC.  The overall analyses are considered to be
only rough estimates since the SWCs' investments include dozens of cities
for which only preliminary feasibility reports are available.   Further-
more, it is difficult to exclude costs unrelated to incremental benefits,
or to determine the average design horizon (year after which volume sold
and population benefited are no longer results of the project) from the
consolidated information.  The final feasibility study of each subproject
in medium and small cities will follow the agreed criteria for proper
evaluation based on marginal cost methodology (para. 6.06).
8.05       The analysis uses a shadow exchange rate factor of 1.15 and a
shadow labor rate of 0.90.   In some cities,  some small investments not
related to incremental benefits (such as pipe replacement, maintenance
equipment or studies for future projects) have been excluded from the
analysis. The results of the analysis of the margina:L cost for water supply
at an opportunity cost of capital of 11% in January 1981 prices are sum-
marized in Table 8.1. The results for the capital cities are satisfactory:
(a) the investment per equivalent person served 1/ averages US$100 per
capita; (b) the marginal cost per cubic meter sold averages US$0.20; and
(c) the covenanted average tariffs per cubic meter sold are about 110% of
the marginal cost.  Rough estimates of the overall marginal cost for the
SWC (Table 8.1) show that present tariff levels are close (92%) to the
marginal cost while the covenanted tariffs during project execution would
be even closer (99%).   This indicates that average tariffs would have
satisfactory levels in terms of resource allocation, even though minimum
consumption charges are subsidized to make them affordable to the urban
poor (para. 7.16). Excess consumption is charged at tariffs about 50-80%
higher than the average.
8.06       The marginal cost for Campo Grande, the capital of Mato Grosso do
Sul, is relatively high (US$0.31/m3). The average covenanted tariffs are
72% of the marginal cost, while excess consumption is 120% of the marginal
cost.  The marginal cost and the investment per equivalent person served 1/
(US$179) are high because of expensive water sources (para. 5.04) and the
effect of investments in distribution that under present rationing are
assumed to result  in additional sales only after 1983.   Nevertheless,
thousands of persons would be served through the new connections, but the
amount  of water  provided would be  somewhat  limited.   It is therefore
acceptable to include the Campo Grande subproject in the project although
it does not comply with the agreed eligibility criteria (para 6.06). For
the   same   reasons   the   rate   of   return   for   Campo   Grande   under
1/ The number of persons served increases annually until the project design
horizon is reached. The equivalent number of persons served is the annual
number (at a discount rate of 11%), which would be served during the life
of the project.

the covenanted tariffs would be 8% for water supply, which is satisfactory
since many economic benefits cannot be quantified (para. 8.01). The rate
of return in Brasilia (9%) is relatively low because of the high ratio
between kilometers of pipelines and connections resulting from its urban
layout, and of past grants (and low debt service) requiring low tariffs for
financial viability.
8.07      Rates of return for the capital cities and for the SWCs as a
whole have been calculated using covenanted tariff levels as a proxy for
economic benefits (Table 8.2).   The forecasted tariff levels and volume
sold are considered feasible by the Bank and the Government. The rate of
return obtained are satisfactory. The weighted average rate of return for
investments in the capital cities (representing 78% of the total project)
is 13% for water and 12% for water and sewerage.  Rough estimates of the
rate of return on total investment at covenanted tariff levels are 10% for
water supply and 10% for water and sewerage.
8.08      These results are moderately sensitive to increases in project
cost or decreases in benefits. For the capital cities, the rate of return
for water supply decreases to 12% with an increase of 10% on project cost,
and to 10% if projects' benefits are reduced 10% simultaneously (Table
8.2).   Higher variations are unlikely.   The use of the present tariff
levels as a proxy for economic benefits reduce the weighted average rate of
return for water on the capital cities to 12%, and to 8% for the SWCs,
showing that tariff increases are required in some states, particularly
Mato Grosso do Sul and the Federal District. The rate of return calculated
using tariffs reflect only a part of the project benefits.  The results of
the rate of return can get closer to the economic rate of return of the
project if the consumer surplus is included.  Bank experience shows that
when necessary, the population is willing to pay much higher prices for
smaller volumes of water. In the project SWCs, water purchased from street
vendors by urban poor for consumption of 20-30 1 per capita reaches prices
4 to 6 times higher than the present average tariffs. At about four times
the average price for this low consumption, the benefits measured by the
rate of return are only about 45% of the total benefits, including the
consumer surplus (Annex 11). The weighted average rate of return for water
supply, including consumer surplus (Table 8.2), is estimated at 23% for the
capital cities and 17% for the SWCs.
Population Benefited and Urban Poverty Impact
8.09       It is estimated that by 1984, the project will serve about 2.5
million new consumers with piped water, increasing population coverage
from 60% (in 1980) to 73%, and the number of persons served by 52% (Table
8.4).   The project will also provide present users with improved water
quality, service pressure and reliability . By 1984, the project will also

extend sewerage services to about 0.6 million new consumers, increasing
the coverage from 15% to 21%, and increasing the number of persons served
by 39%. Follow-up sewerage projects will be studied during project execu-
tion. After project completion, the benefits and population served will
continue to grow. The small investments required to achieve this would be
partially financed by users' contributions. A rough estimate of the equi-
valent number of persons to be served during the total project's useful
life is about 4.6 million 1/ for water and 1.0 million for sewerage.
8.10       In the capital cities, the project will provide water to about
1.25 million new consumers,2/ increasing the coverage from 67% in 1980 to
81% by 1984, (increasing the number of persons served by 43%). During the
same period, sewerage services will serve 0.5 million new consumers, in-
creasing the coverage from 20% to 30%, (increasing the number of persons
served by 54%).
8.11      The impact of the project on the poverty groups,  defined as
families earning three minimum salaries (ORTN $9.2 or US$80 per month) or
less was estimated for the capital cities (Table 8.3).  About 60% of the
urban poor will have water connections by 1984. About 785,000 or about 63%
of the new customers to be benefitted from the project belong to the
poverty group.   Sewerage investments are limited to densely populated
areas, where reliance on individual solutions such as septic tanks and
latrines is not feasible.   About 48,000 urban poor (10% of the incre
mental population to be served) will be included among sewerage subproject
beneficiaries in the capital cities.
Affordability of Project Services
8.12      The Tariff Law specifies that the minimum monthly charge for
water should not exceed 35% of one ORTN (US$4.06), and the combined charge
for water and sewerage should not exceed 50% of one ORTN (US$5.80). The
existing monthly charges for minimum consumption of water range from 21% to
68% (average: 46%) of the values allowed by the Tariff Law. Furthermore,
minimum water charges are only about 1.8% of the minimum salary (Annex
8.5). As many urban poor families earn more than one minimum salary, water
is affordable to the poor.   Connection charges, which are very low, are
being phased out by BNH making water services accessible to all.   The
combined water and sewerage charges are 57% of the values allowed by the
Tariff Law and represent only about 3.1% of the minimum salary.  Under the
covenanted tariffs, the minimum water charges, even if increased in pro-
portion to the average tariff increases, would remain well below Tariff Law
allowances and below 3% of the minimum salary, which is satisfactory.
1/ See para. 8.05, footnote 1/.
2/ The equivalent number of persons served during the project's
useful life will be almost twice (2.34 million).



- 52 -
Project Risks
8.13      The project presents only minor technical risks since most of
the works do not involve special engineering requirements, and the country
has a well developed engineering and construction industry. Furthermore,
final designs are well advanced, and their progress will be closely moni-
tored during project execution by Bank and BNH staff. Cost escalation to
levels that would jeopardize the project's worth are very unlikely. Since
tariffs are generally low, any unforseeable cost increase could be ba-
lanced by increases in tariffs. Overall, the project presents lower than
average risks. BNH's supervisory role will be greatly enhanced during this
project. The transfer of new responsibilities to BNH for project implemen-
tation and supervision will have to be progressive, however. The benefi-
ciaries of the project are generally weaker than in previous loans to BNH,
and the project scope is equivalent to six average projects in the sector.
Bank supervision efforts (para. 6.22) would be critical, especially during
the first two years, when BNH staff is being trained and the SWCs are being
familiarized with Bank procedures. Otherwise, there would be a substan-
tial risk that the institutional improvements would not be achieved and
that implementation of the works would be delayed.
IX. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
9.01      During loan negotiations assurances were obtained from the Fed-
eral Government that:
(a)  the Government will provide or cause BNH to provide the
required counterpart funds.  Counterpart funds needs will
be analyzed with the Bank at least once a year (para. 2.11);
and
(b)  the revenue covenant will be met (para. 7.15) by all the
SWCs included in the proposed project.
9.02      Assurances were obtained from BNH that as a condition for dis-
bursement in the respective SWC the project State Governments will provide
written assurances to the Bank that they will provide the required counter-
part funds (para. 6.17).
9.03      During loan negotiations assurances were obtained from BNH that:
(a)  counterpart funds will be analyzed with the Bank and the
Government at least once a year and provided as needed
(para. 2.11);
(b)  Bank's procurement guidelines and procurement schedule will
be followed (para. 3.15);
(c)  external auditors reports for BNH and each SWC, including
compliance vith financial covenants and in the case of BNH
the auditor's opinion on the reliability of Statements of
Expenditures, will be presented annually to the Bank (para.
4.13);
(d) waste disposal Master Plan studies will be made for Manaus,
Belem, Goiania and Campo Grande (para. 5.11);



- 53 -
(e) the subproject selection criteria will be applied to all
subprojects being financed under this loan (para. 6.06);
the subproject selection criteria will be extended to all
projects financed by BNH by December 31, 1981 (para. 3.13);
(f) BNH will make biannual field supervisions and prepare re-
ports to the Bank (para. 6.22);
(g) procurement advertisements will be made annually
(para. 6.23);
(h) SWCs revenues will meet the revenue covenant (para. 7.15);
(i) four additional staff members will be hired for COSAN not
later than December 31, 1981 (para. 3.05); and
(j) a completion report will be prepared (para. 7.29).
9.04       Assurances were obtained from BNH that as a condition of dis-
bursements for each SWC agreements satisfactory to the Bank will be signed
between the project State Water Company,  the State Government and BNH
(para.  6.18).    Such  agreements  will  incorporate  all  loan  conditions
related to each SWC including the following:
(a)  a personnel system will be implemented not later than Dec-
ember 31, 1982 (para. 4.09);
(b) a system analysis unit will be created not later than Dec-
ember 31, 1982 (para. 4.11);
(c) financial statements will be audited annually (para. 4.13);
(d) internal audit offices will be established not later than
December 31, 1982 (para 4.15);
(e) waste disposal Master Plans will be made for Manaus, Belem,
Goiania and Campo Grande (para. 5.11);
(f) metering levels in major cities will reach 70% in 1982 and
80% in 1983 (para. 5.10);
(g) fixed assets of the SWCs will be reassessed (para. 7.12);
(h) the revenue covenant will be met (para. 7.15);
(i) financial projections will be presented annually to the
Bank (para. 7.29);



- 54 -
(j)  semiannual reports and monitoring indicators will be sent
to the Bank for review (para. 7.29); and
(k) a completion report will be prepared (para. 7.29).
9.05      The following are conditions for effectiveness:
(a)  the signing of the first agreement satisfactory to the Bank
between one SWC, the respective State Government and BNH
(para. 6.18);
(b)  the appointment by BNH of the first three additional staff
members for COSAN (para. 3.05); and
(c)  the signing of the contract for COSAN technical assistance
(para. 3.05).
9.06      Retroactive financing for US$7 million  (3.9% of the proposed
loan) is recommended for urgently needed works (para. 6.28).
9.07      The project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$180.0 mil-
lion. The loan would be for a period of 15 years, including a grace period
of three years.

BRAZIL
MULTISTATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
PLANASA EVALUATION
1.01      The PLANASA program has been changed during its implementation,
adapting its programs to the experience obtained.  Operations are partly
decentralized and measures have been taken to improve the investment plan-
ning, the quality of engineering studies, and the organization and manage-
ment of the SWCs. The organization and operation of PLANASA are described
in Chapter III.
1.02      PLANASA has proven to be a well conceived system which covers
every legal, financial, management, and organization aspect. The results
achieved are impressive, considering that in 1961 the level of water and
sewerage services was the second lowest in Latin America.  The number of
cities or towns integrated to PLANASA grew from 357 in 1972 to 2,007 in
1978 (67% are communities with less than 5,000 inhabitants), while the
number of households connected increased from 3.1 million to 8.1 million.
PLANASA's investment between 1968-80 was around US$5.2 billion (Table 1)
of which about 70% for water.   Investments per urban inhabitants have
increased continuously (Table 1) and are now about US$14 per person.
1.03      Within PLANASA, BNH has established a technical assistance pro-
gram to strengthen the SWCs. As a result of this program, most of them are
using computerized systems for accounting, billing, inventories, fixed
assets,  etc.   Training has been provided to about half of the 80,000
employees of the SWCs.l/ About 27 books, 5 manual and 67 technical films
have been produced.  Ninety consultant firms work in the design of pro-
jects, while 550 construction firms with some 105,000 employees execute
the works.
1.04      The  PLANASA  implementation  program  is progressing,  although
slower than expected.   All of the 23 states of Brazil have joined the
program and more than 2,100 municipalities are now benefitted from it.
About 70% of the urban population is now receiving piped water compared
with less than 60% of a smaller urban population when the government
started the program in 1971.2/ It is estimated that PLANASA's water supply
1/ As part of the PLANASA program, BNH has organized a national sanitation
training program to improve the qualifications of both professional and
nonprofessional staff working in the sector.   BNH's target is to train
annually about 20% of the 80,000 SWC employees.
2| Source: Censo Nacional de Saneamento Basico 1978-Resultados Prelimi-
nares BNH-February 1979.



- 67 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 6
objectives will be met only around 1985.  The levels of service attained
and projected for the provision of sewerage services present a different
picture.  At present, about 28% of the population is served as compared
with 30% in 1971 when PLANASA began.   This decline of sewerage service
levels is the result of:  (i) priority given by the Government to water
supply;  (ii)  technical  complexity  of building  and operating  adequate
sewerage systems in marginal areas of the cities which lack urban planning:
(iii) relatively high per capita cost of providing sewerage services and
the existence of other low cost alternatives; and (iv) high rate of urban
population growth.  It is estimated that PLANASA objectives for sewerage
services will not be met until 1990.
1.05      In order to strengthen the execution capabilitieVof the SWCs,
BNH entered into an agreement with PAHO to carry out SATECIA,- a technical
assistance program. SATECIA program was executed between 1974 and 1980,
and provided assistance to all SWCs.  Many improvements in tariffs, ac-
counting and methods resulted from this assistance. The program was how-
ever expensive and did not fulfill BNH's expectations in relation to over-
all results, partly because of the time necessary for foreign consultants
to adapt to the language and idiosyncrasy of Brazil.   To continue the
technical assistance to the SWCs after February 1980, BNI has created
PRODISAN (Subprograma de Desenvolvimento Institucional das Companhias
Estaduais de Saneamento). This subprogram aims to provide assistance for
institution building and training to the SWCs coordinated through BNH. BNH
retained four persons of the SATECIA's staff to assist in the organization
and start-up of PRODISAN.
1.06      The main focus of the PLANASA program has been to help the states
develop their own capable and strong organizations in the water supply and
sewerage sector. To enter the program, a state must sign an agreement with
BNH establishing a statewide water and sewerage company, draw up a state-
wide sector investment plan and provide for a state contribution to invest-
ments and for the guarantee of BNH loans.  The statewide organization of
the program has four major advantages.   First,  it makes possible the
establishment of statewide tariff structures which permit a cross-subsidi-
zation mechanism within the state. In this way, wealthier cities contri-
bute to support poorer and small communities. Second, the statewide orga-
nization derives economies of scale from centralized management of the
state systems.   Third, it makes possible the development by the SWCs of
modern management systems, and fourth, the financial capacity of the
cities is enhanced since cash deficits and surpluses are shared.
1/ SATECIA: Programa de Asistencia Tecnica para o Desenvolvimento Insti-
tucional das Empresas Estaduais de Saneamento.



- 68 -
ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 6
1.07      The PLANASA Program is financed one-half by BNH and one-half by
the states through revolving water and sewerage funds (Fundos de Agua e
Esgotos FAEs). The participation of the state is critical for the program.
The degree of commitment with the sector and the resources of each state
have been largely different.   FAE resources are obtained from budgetary
allocations of up to 5% of the state's annual tax revenues.  In cases where
5% of a state's revenues is not sufficient to meet the state's 50% financ-
ing commitment to the program, BNH extends supplementary loans to the state
to cover this shortfall (FINEST I).  If the state allocates less than 5% of
its revenues to the program, the amount it can borrow from BNH is reduced
proportionally.
1.08      In addition to the basic lending programs for water (REFINAG)
and sewerage (REFINESG), BNH has also two subprograms (FINEST 2 and FINEST
3) geared to stimulate investments in small communities (less than 5,000
inhabitants). Under these subprograms BNH's loans are made to the states
through their financial agents and are equal to the states' funds allocated
to the small communities program. Under both subprograms the SWCs receive
directly from the states part of the funds as capital contributions (about
70%) and the rest as FAE's loans. BNH's loans to the states under FINEST 2
are aimed at replenishing the states' contributions to FAE, and thus are
limited to the amount of such foregone contributions. Under FINEST 3, BNH
loans to the states constitute an equivalent and supplementary source of
funds for small communities, thus permitting to double the amount of funds
allocated to these communities. In addition to the funds received by SWC
from BNH through COSAN, there is a line of credit to developers to finance
sanitary works for housing programs (FINEC).
1.09      BNH's lending terms for the sanitation sector reflect social,
rather than commercial, considerations.   Interest rates on the REFINAG,
REFINESG and FINEST I lines of credit vary from 2.0% p.a. to 7.0%.  The
lower rates are applicable to the poorer states. Interest rate concessions
have been granted for sewerage projects and related supplemental lending
programs (FINEST II and III). The grace period on BNH's sanitation loans
are from 18 to 54 months.  The amortization period is 18 years for water
supply loans and 30 years for sewerage loans.  Effective March 28, 1980,
BNH reduced the interest rates on all new FAE loans for water supply from
the previous 3%-6% level to 2%-2.5% and on all new FAE sewerage loans to a
flat 2%. For their services the OT charges 1.2% on each of BNH disburse-
ments. BNH/FAE finances 110% of the direct cost of contracts to include
the estimated 10% engineering and project supervision costs. The interest
on BNH and FAE loans for the project SWCs is generally lower than for many
other SWCs because per capita income of the population in the project

1.10      BNH's amortization periods reflect the useful lives of the sub-
projects financed and are considerably longer than the terms of Bank loans.
This, and the relative movement between exchange rates and the revaluation
index of the ORTN may cause the effective interest rate on the Bank loan to
exceed the onlending rates charged by BNH. Their resulting risk, as well
as the exchange rate risk would be assumed by BNH where financial position
(and higher interest rates in other loans) is strong enough to absorb both.
1.11      PLANASA should be improved during this project.  Major factors
affecting PLANASA's past performance have been:
(a) Resource Allocation
Unclear sector priorities at the national level, lack of econo-
mic analysis of projects, use of questionable techniques for
least-cost analysis and project justification, and high per ca-
pita costs.  Sound economic analysis techniques will be imple-
mented during the project (para. 3.13).
(b) Financial
Unclear definition of financial objectives for the SWCs in the
period 1979-82, low tariffs which are not fully adjusted for
inflation, high operating costs and debt service in the SWCs.
Additionally, in some states the extended poverty of the popu-
lation makes it difficult to generate enough revenues to cover
financial needs. During the project, the methodology of the EVG
will be improved and a general revenue covenant (para. 7.15)
will provide satisfactory financial viability.



- 71 -
ANNEX 3
Page 6 of 6
(c) Political
Political interference in the SWCs, which prevents the proper
application of a national system of priorities for the sector,
reluctance by national and municipal institutions to transfer
cities to the jurisdiction of PLANASA, and excessive turnover of
the high management of the SWCs. The implementation of a system
of functions, and responsibilities and salaries (para.   4.09)
would reduce the effects of these problems.
(d) Technical
Instability of the SWCs' investment programs, overestimation of
demands for services, oversizing of systems, underestimation of
project costs and overoptimistic execution schedules. The pro-
posed guidelines for subproject analysis and EVG preparation are
expected to eliminate these problems.
(e) Personnel
Slow processing of BNH loans and difficulties in getting quali-
fied personnel in some regions. BNH's personnel constraints for
proper supervision,  planning and project evaluation.   BNH is
studying several Bank recommendations to simplify its proce-
dures. COSAN personnel will the strengthened and technical as-
sistance will provide advice on all procedures and methods used
by BNH (para. 3.05).
COSAN Evaluation
1.12      COSAN has showed great dynamism and capacity to provide a sound
financial conceptual framework.  Other strong points on COSAN's perform-
ance have been its capacity and flexibility to adapt to the changing
environment in the country, its political independence and technical capa-
city, and an impressive experience dealing with subprojects in hundreds of
cities. COSAN staff has, however, been overwhelmed by the magnitude of the
problems in dealing with all SWCs and subprojects in Brazil and BNH's
cumbersome procedures. Additional experienced and qualified personnel is
urgently needed but has been difficult to hire because of Government and
BNH ceilings in staff and salaries. Sector information is not centralized
or processed, and the coordination with regional offices requires improve-
ments. The technical assistance provided through the project (para. 3.05)
will require a flexible approach to avoid the red tape in contracting
qualified consultants. Draft terms of reference for the technical assis-
tance to COSAN have been reviewed by the Bank and proposals would be
requested at the beginning of 1981. COSAN staff reinforcement require high
caliber professionals. The Bank has provided COSAN with a profile of the
professionals required.

BRAZIL
MULTI-STATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
PROPOSED WORKS AND OTHER COMPONENTS
This Annex briefly describes the proposed works in each of the cities.
The investment schedule is given in Annex 6. Annual investments during the
construction period are given in Annex 5. The following are the components
of the different subprojects:
1. AMAZONAS
A. WATER SUPPLY MANAUS
a) expansion of the existing water treatment plant from a capacity of
2,000 I/s to about 3,000 l/s;
b) expansion of the existing low and high pressure pumping stations
from a capacity of 2,600 l/s to 4,000 l/s;
c) construction of a 19 km treated water transmission line;
d) improvements and expansions of the water distribution system in-
cluding the construction and/or the installation of:
i)   two storage tanks with a combined capacity of 23,000 m3;
ii) 170 km of pipes;
iii) 26,000 house connections; and
iv) 40,000 consumption water meters.
e) production metering and a leak detection program.
B. WATER SUPPLY MEDIUM SIZE CITIES
a) expansion of the water supply systems of the cities of Tabatinga
(estimated 1980 population: 8,000), and Labrea (estimated 1980 popu-
lation: 5,000); and
b) improvements to various water systems.



- 73 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 8-
C. WATER SUPPLY SMALL COMMUNITIES
Construction or improvement of water supply systems in the follow-
ing towns:   Novo Airao, Atalaia Norte, Barcelos,  Borba,  Constant,
Canutama, Fonte Boa, Japura, Jutai, Marao, Novo Aripuana, Pauini, Sta.
Isabel, San Antonio do Ica, S. Paulo, Tapana, Anori, and Beruri.
D. EQUIPMENT
Purchase of equipment for the operation and maintenance of water
systems and of sewerage.
E. SEWERAGE
a) Construction of about 160 km sewers; and
b) improvements in the existing sewerage.
F. ENGINEERING AND TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
a) construction supervision;
b) completion of studies and final design of the Manaus water system;
and
c) technical assistance and administration.
2. FEDERAL DISTRICT
A. WATER SUPPLY BRASILIA METROPOLITAN
a) improvements in the Santa Maria water supply system;
b) construction of the first stage of the Rio Descoberto water treat-
ment plant with a capacity of about 2,000 l/s;
c)   improvements and expansions in the water distribution systems
including the construction and/or installation of:
i)   870 km of pipes (about 250 km in Brasilia downtown, and 620 km
in other neighborhoods);
ii) 23,000 house connections; and
iii) 44,000 consumption water meters.

a) Construction of about 350 km sewers;

installing tertiary treatment facilities (nitrogen removal); and
c) construction of a sewage treatment plant (oxidation ponds) to treat
sewage effluents from Taguatinga and Ceilandia.

and sewerage systems.

Jardim, Nova Andradina, Fatima, Aquidanana, Corumba/Ladario, Ponta
Pora, and Tres Lagoas.

BRAZIL
MULTISTATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Disbursement Procedures
Introduction
1.        As in previous loans to BNH, disbursements will be made against
statements of expenditures. This is the only practical procedure under the
circumstances where the loan is made to the financial intermediary, where
there are a large number of subprojects financed, and where contracts for
both supply and construction are generally awarded to local contractors.
The present disbursement procedure is consistent with BNH's procedures,
enables a proper control over Bank disbursements, is working satisfac-
torily in ongoing projects. The only modification proposed refers to the
establishment  of a working fund account (para.  8).   The BNH financing
mechanism and the Bank reimbursement procedures are described below.
BNH/SWC Financing Contract
2.        When any subproject  foreseen  in the Loan Agreement has been
approved by BNH, and the Bank if over the free limit, the submitting SWC
requests a PLANASA financing contract for that subproject (it may combine
two or more subprojects in a single request). This request (solicitacao de
financiamento) is submitted to BNH's financial agent, which is the state
bank in each state and also handles the state FAE.   The financial agent
then submits the request to BNH and to FAE, and formal loan agreements are
prepared establishing terms, total amount of the loan, amount of the ad-
vance payment, and repayment terms. This contract is between BNH and the
financial agent and specifies the SWC and the subproject or subprojects for
which the funds are provided. The financial agent, in turn, makes ident-
ical contracts with the SWC.   For control purposes,  BNH establishes a
financial contract number (CTN) for each contract and all subsequent pay-
ment requests must bear that number. The financing and service flows for
the SWCs is shown below:

Borrower's (BNH) Expenditure Control Procedure
3.        Expenditure control for BNH is exercised in the SWC as follows:
(a) bidding documents and final contracts for civil works,
equipment and services to be rendered for the project are
examined by the BNH to verify that the contracts do, in fact,
pertain to the subproject. the technical consultans of BNH
(OTs) review all invoices submitted against those contracts
to establish their validity as to percentage of completion,
delivery of materials, and also monitor the construction of
all subprojects; and
(b) the financial agent verifies the authenticity of documenta-
tion and verifies paid invoices against advance payment re-
quests.
BNH Disbursements
4.        Actual disbursements on the part of BNH are in two parts, an
initial advance (stipulated in the financing contract) and subsequent pay-
ments until the project is completed.   Subsequent payments may be made
against paid or unpaid invoices. A description of these systems follows.



- 90 -
ANNEX 7
Page 3 of 4
5.        An amount up to 20% of the total expenditure may be disbursed in
advance of expenditure. To obtain this advance payment, the SWC submits a
letter of request to the financial agent. The financial agent prepares a
request to BNH, attaching the SWC's letter and submits it to BNH for
payment. Based on this, BNH emits a payment order (Autorizacao de Paga-
mento para Aplicacao), which is paid to the financial agent who credits it
to the SWC's account after deducting the administrative and technical
service fees.   The advance payment is recovered by deducting from each
subsequent payment the same percentage that the advance represented to the
total contract. In the event that the subprojects (s) is (are) terminated
without expending the total amount of financing, any advance payment re-
maining is simply added to the loan total for amortization. In the event
of overruns,  the advance is automatically  liquidated and overruns are
generally financed by a supplementary financial contract.
6.        The SWC has two payment options.  Under the first alternative,
the SWC submits a list of invoices that it has paid, together with the
invoices, to the technical agent of BNH for review. The OT approves (or
rejects) each individual invoice, correcting the listing for rejections,
and returns all documents to the SWC.  The SWC then submits the approved
listing to the financial agent who verifies that they were paid and then
submits the list of paid invoices to BNH and FAE for reimbursement. Under
the second alternative the procedure is identical except that a list of
unpaid invoices is submitted and the request is one for an advance payment,
supported by the list of paid invoices to prove that the previous month's
advance was used to pay the invoices for which the advance was requested.
Processing of both lists is identical, and they are processed as a single
submission.
7.        On receipt of the verified list of paid invoices from the finan-
cial agent, BNH issues a payment order for 50% of the value of the request
to which the list of paid invoices is attached. This document is approved
by COSAN, and then routed to BNH's Finance Department for the payment
authorization and to have funds transferred to the financial agent. Admi-
nistrative and technical service fees are deducted.  The financial agent
then credits the appropriate account of the SWC.
Basic of Transactions between BNH, the Final User and the Bank
8.        The basis of project approval, the financial contracts, and BNH
disbursements is the UPC (Unidade Padrao de Capital). The UPC is a mone-
tary unit which is adjusted every three months in accordance with the
percentage increase granted to Federal Government Bonds (ORTN). All dis-
bursement requests by the SWCs, final contract settlements, and payments
of interest and principal are stated in terms of UPC at the conversion rate
to the cruzeiro in effect in the month in which the SWC makes the request.
BNH submits to the Bank requests for reimbursements for expenditures on



- 91
ANNEX 7
Page 4 of 4
approved subprojects (CTNs) supported by the listing of paid invoices (or
unpaid invoices as the case may be).   BNH through its OTs and auditing
staff exercises satisfactory control over subproject implementation and
over disbursements to contractors. The accounting procedures followed are
straightforward and the records will be available for supervision missions
to verify expenditures on selected projects. Furthermore, under the loan
the independent auditor of the SWCs will be required to certify that the
disbursement procedures are properly being followed by the SWC.
9.        In order to reduce the interval during which BNH would finance
the Bank's share of project cost with its own resources, and the under-
disbursement of the Bank loan under high local inflation rates, BNH may
request the Bank to make advance payments from the loan account into a
Special Account, to be opened in US dollars in the Banco Central do Brasil,
and would be available for finarncing only the Bank's share of the project
cost.   The amount deposited in the Special Account may be increased or
decreased by the Bank as required for project execution but should not
exceed US$12 million.  BNH would be entitled to make withdrawals from the
Special Account at the exchange rate applicable on the day the expenditures
were made. Loan disbursements will be made against a statement of expendi-
tures prepared by BNH for each subproject, the detailed documentation of
which will not be submitted for Bank review, but will be retained by the
beneficiary and made available for inspection during the course of super-
vision missions.  No works or force account are envisaged.  Disbursement
procedures are explained in Annex 7. The estimated schedule of disburse-
ments, to be completed before Jtne 30, 1985, is shown in Annex 20.
