BRAZIL
MULTISTATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
STATE WATER COMPANIES (SWC) FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
Notes on existing Financial Statements
1.01      All  the SWCs use a standardized  and computarized  accounting
system.   The reliability of the information is, however,  low, regarding
particularly the distribution of expenditures between the capital and the
other cities, and the segregation of costs between water and sewerage.
Historical financial statements are based on reports prepared by the ex-
ternal auditors retained by the SWCs at BNH's request. The quality of the
auditing varies also in quality, but is in general acceptable. The analy-
sis of historical statements is further complicated by the way monetary
corrections on fixed assets and liabilities are presented in accordance
with the law. This presentation distorts the income and cash flow state-
ments for purposes of cost control, rate making and comparison of financial
performance among SWCs.
1.02      High inflation rates prevailing in Brazil (about 110% in 1980)
further complicate the analysis of the SWCs financial performance: with
these levels of inflation the sum of monetary values within the same year
no longer is meaningful. Under these circumstances, and because of uncer-
tainty  regarding  inflation  ffyecasts  it  is preferable  to present  SWC
financial statements in ORTN.- Projections in ORTN have the additional
advantage that the fixed assets are by law maintained in ORTN and that the
SWC debt service payments are expressed in UPC.   On the other hand, the
ORTN value for the year constitutes only a gross approximation. Further-
more the projection in ORTN (or any hard currency) tends to underestimate
the cash needs of a SWC, since the current assets, not subject to monetary
correction would rapidly lose their value under high inflation rates. In
order to reflect this loss in the financial projections, a provision has
been included in the non-operating expenses.
Financial Projections-Inflation
1.03      Until 1973 the ORTN was kept in line with general inflation.
Thereafter, the ORTN index became an instrument for monetary policy. The
increase in general prices as measured by the Indice Geral dos Precos (IGP)
of the Getulio Vargas Foundation was higher than the increase of the ORTN
by 6.7% in 1976, 6.6% in 1975, 3.4% in 1978, 20.5% in 1979 and 40% in 1980.
Starting in 1981, the Government intends to maintain a closer relationship
1/ The ORTN (Obrigacoes Reajustaveis do Tesouro Nacional) is the monetary
adjustment index for government bonds. For each quarter the UPC is main-
tained at a value equal to the ORTN of the first month (January, April,
July, October).



_ 9c _
ANNEX 9
Page 2 of 24
between the consumer index (INPC of the IBGE) and the ORTN. The INPC was
created  in April 1979 and is being used for salary adjustments.   The
historical differences between the general price index (IGP) and the INPC
(8%-10% per year in favor of the IGP) are expected to continue. A price
contingency of 10% in 1981, 9% in 1982 and 8% in 1983 and thereafter has
been used in all appraisal projections.
EVGs
1.04      BNH requires all SWCs to prepare annually an Estudo de Viabili-
dade Global Economico-Financiero - (EVG). The EVG is a five-year financial
projection in ORTN where, demand, investment and operating costs are pre-
sented for each city, and where income, tariffs and annual rates of return
are consolidated for the SWC as a whole.   The Bank has been providing
assistance to BNH to improve the EVG preparation, particularly the finan-
cial, economic and social analysis and the incorporation of full financial
statements, including the effect of general price increases measured in
ORTN. The 1980 EVGs for all the SWCs were reviewed during the appraisal,
discussed with the SWCs and BNH and their results, substantially revised,
have been incorporated in the financial projections (pages 7 to 24). The
EVGs prepared for 1981 will incorporate Bank recommendations.
1.05      The EVG is a very important planning tool, which needs further
improvements. The EVG provides also projections of FAE and state funds for
project execution. The implementation of sound guidelines for least cost
and economic evaluation (para. 6.06) should optimize the investments con-
sidered in the EVG.
Demand
1.06      The population and population growth are only gross estimates.
The results of the 1980 Census will only be available by mid-1981. Popu-
lation estimates by the IBGE have been used, when available, and have been
complemented with the results of surveys made by the SWCs, consultants, and
other state institutions.
1.07      The percentage of population served is likely to be under esti-
mated. The number of persons per household should be somehow higher than
in the 1970 census, although the number of persons per family is likely to
be lower because construction of new houses has not coped with the growing
demand for dwellings, and concentration of population in existing ones
should result.  In addition, the number of illegal connections is consi-
dered important in several cities; users' surveys are being carried out to
eliminate illegal connections in major cities.
1.08      Water demand projections in subproject feasibility reports and
EVGs are not adequate. Projections normally use per capita demand without



- 96 -
ANNEX 9
Page 3 of 24
considering levels of unaccounted-for water, income of the population to
be served, effect of industrial, public or commercial demand and the exist-
ence or nonexistence of metering. Water demand statistics are prepared but
seldom used in forecasting.   Furthermore, lack of proper tariff adjust-
ments have induced some SWCs to create sophisticated methods to inflate the
water billed to unmetered connections. In Para, and especially in Goias,
the application of such methods reduce the reported unaccounted-for water
by about half, distort billing and reduce artificially the need for meter-
ing.   BNH and the Government are now aware of these problems and are
prepared to be correct them gradually. An important action towards reach-
ing this goal is metering: metering levels have been covenanted at 80% for
all major cities. Water demand projections have been prepared on the basis
of available statistical information for 1979 and mid-1980.
1.09      Unaccounted-for water is very high in the SWCs of Amazonas, Para
and Paraiba. Leak detection, metering and users' surveys are part of the
proposed project and should reduce the unaccounted-for water to about 30%
in the capital cities by 1984.
1.10      When one connection serves several apartments, the total volume
billed is divided between the number of apartments or "economias". These
"economias" are used as units of reference for all statistical purposes. On
the SWCs of Amazonas and Paraiba most connections serve only one residence;
the same happens in Para (with the exception of Belem). In these SWCs the
statistics for connections were considered reliable and meaningful and
were used for demand projections. In the SWCs of Goias, Mato Grosso do Sul
and the Federal District the unit used for demand projections was the
economia, but the general name "connections" appear in all financial pro-
jections.
Operating Expenses
1.11      Operation and maintenance expenses have been projected separa-
tely for water and sewerage. Unit wages are adjusted using the INPC index
plus about 3% p.a., which the Government allows for merit increases (pro-
ductividade). The biannual variation of the INPC is multiplied by 1.1 for
personnel earning less than three minimum salaries, by 1 for employees
earning 3 to 10 minimum salaries and by 0.8 for other employees.   Total
personnel expenditures used in projections assume an increase in producti-
vity in all SWCs (Annex 12, monitoring indicators), i.e., a decrease in the
ratio of employees per 1000 connections (especially in Goias).
1.12      Power usage is expected to increase in line with volume pro-
duced, but at unit rates slightly higher than inflation. Nevertheless, in
Para the starting of the Tucurui Power plant is assumed to reduce power
costs by 1982. Materials, services and administrative costs are increased
in accordance with inflation and in varying proportion with the number of
water and sewerage connections. The proportion is different for each SWC



- 97 -
ANNEX 9
Page 4 of 24
and depends on the estimated economies of scale, improvement introduced in
the present water distribution system and the growing proportion of new
assets (which should require less maintenance). High inflation and lack of
interest charges on delayed payments make meaningless the legal provision
for bad debts (3% of accounts receivable); a provision of 1% of total
billing is being added to the operating expenses.
Investments-Financing
1.13      Project and other investments include technical and price con-
tingencies and about 10% for general administrative and engineering ex-
penses. This percentage, which is financed by BNH, should be reviewed from
time to time to see if its reduction is justified.
1.14      In practice all investments are financed by loans or grants and
internal contribution to investment have been very small. In the projec-
tions, each investment is assumed to be financed half by BNH and half by
FAE, including their respective interest and other charges during con-
struction.   For the SWCs which execute simultaneously annual subprojects
in 50 or more cities, this method would require the projection on hundreds
of different loans during the projection period.  A simplified approach,
consolidating all these loans in a few per year have been used in the
financial projections. BNH management is studying the implementation of
such system to simplify their lending procedures. Interest and other debt
charges have been estimated at present rates of BNH and FAE; the grace
period is up to four and a half years and the principal repayment is 18
years for water and 30 years for sewerage.
Tariff
1.15      Water and sewerage average tariffs have been projected to meet
the proposed revenue covenant (para 7.15).   It is assumed that at least
twice a year, tariff will be reviewed and tariff adjustments made to
maintain such levels throughout each year. The level of revenues that the
tariff law would enable the SWCs to achieve satisfactory financial per-
formance. Existing Bank revenue covenants vary widely among the different
projects 1/ and are difficult to monitor. For consistency within the sec-
tor, it is recommended that the same revenue covenant be used for all SWCs
under Bank projects.
1.16      Other operational revenues include connections and installation
fees, and in Brasilia the "pollution tax" which is equivalent to 10% of the
1/  Loan 1525-BR:   Revenues sufficient to cover operating expenses, debt
service and 1.5% of net assets in 1981, 2.5% in 1982 and 3.5% thereafter,
(SABESP); Loan 1656-BR:   rate of return of 6% in 1981 and 1982, and 7%
thereafter (COMPESA); Loans 1823-BR and 1850-BR:   tariff adjustments in
gradual increases in real terms to assure that tariffs will reach in 1983
the level required by the Brazilian law (CORSAN, CASAN, SANEPAR and
COPASA).



- 98 -
ANNEX 9
Page 5 of 24
water and sewerage billing. A fine, representing 10% of billing, is charged
only once to consumers in arrears for more than one month.   This charge,
which is considered to only partially compensate for inflation losses, is
normally excluded from the operating revenues.
Balance statements
1.17      Cash:  the minimum level of cash is about one month of personnel
costs.   Accounts receivable are net of the provision for bad debt and
should be reduced in several SWCs (Annex 12) to about two months of billing
by 1984.
1.18      Inventories have been evaluated and reduced in some SWCs or
increased to a safe level in others. Inventories of materials being used
for project construction have been included with works in progress.  In
general the level of inventories have been projected in relation to the re-
quirements of materials and other supplies.
1.19      Fixed assets and depreciation are maintained by law in ORTN.
However, when the SWCs were created the historical value of fixed assets
transferred to the SWCs by the municipalities was recorded in books. In-
complete fixed-asset inventories and valuation, lack of correct accumu-
lated depreciation and absence of reliable data on fixed assets contri-
buted by users made theJ/ixed assets records and the estimated rates of
return very inaccurate.- Fixed assets will be reassessed during project
execution.
1.20      The rate of return allowed by the Tariff Law should not exceed
12%. This level is unlikely to be reached under the present BNH and FAE
interest rates and repayment periods.   The rate of return is calculated
dividing net income after depreciation by the average recognized invest-
ment ("investimento reconhecido"), which is different from the asset base
used normally in the Bank. The recognized investment exclude fixed assets
financed by government or users grants, and include a certain percentage of
current assets. In practice the difference is not significative, although
the computation of the "recognized investment" is time consuming.
1.21      Accounts payable corresponding to operating purchases are pro-
jected in relation to operating expenses. Accounts payable to contractors
and suppliers are assumed to represent half to one month of investments.
Accounts payable are excessive in some SWC (especially CAGEPA) and should
achieve reasonable levels by 1982.
1/  In some of the SWCs, gross fixed assets per person served are about
half (US$40/person) of their estimated replacement values, while accumu-
lated depreciation represents only about 5% of the gross assets.   The
magnitude of the new investments, however, maintain a reasonable revalued
value.   In the project SWCs, the total 1980 fixed assets represent only
about 30% of the total 1984 fixed assets.



- 99 -
ANNEX 9
Page 6 of 24
BNH appraisal reports
1.22      During this project, BNH participated in the appraisal for the
first time,and prepared basic analysis and projections (Avaliacao pre-
liminar do projecto)  for the SWCs of SANEAGO,  SANESUL and CAESB.   The
results have been satisfactory. BNH analysis was complemented with pre-
appraisal mission data to make the financial and economic analysis com-
patible with the other SWCs and Bank standards. Recommendations to improve
future BNH appraisal are included in the Project File.
Additional Information
1.23      Full financial projections, including detailed tables on demand,
project cost, debt service, financial assumption, financial statements,
monitoring indicators and economic analysis are available in Project File
for each SWC.

BRAZIL
MULTISTATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Subproject Selection Criteria
1.        All subprojects acceptable for Bank financing should comply with
the following conditions:
(a) they must "oe approved by BNH or included in the last EVG approved
by BNH and accepted by the Bank;
(b) total subproject cost should not be substantially higher than
that included in the EVG.   If subproject  scope or costs are
substantially different (more than 30%) from those contemplated
in the EVG, satisfactory evidence must be presented to BNH and
the Bank that their inclusion and financing will not deteriorate
the SWC's financial position;
(d) subprojects must be the least-cost solution.
2.        Calculations of long-run incremental costs for subproject ac-
ceptability, and of present value and long-run incremental cost for least-
cost analysis, should use an annual discount rate equal to the opportunity
cost of capital (11%) or another value agreed upon between BNH and the
Bank. These calculations will be based on the subprojects' capital costs,
operating costs, and complementary investments.
3.        Water supply subprojects to be carried out in cities with popu-
lations greater than 50,000, must have water tariffs 1/ equal to or higher
than 75% of the long-run average incremental cost. This criteria will be
considered equivalent to an IFR of 5% or higher (i.e. average tariff equal
or higher than the marginal cost discounted at a 5% interest rate).
4.        Sewerage subprojects, or water supply subprojects to be carried
out in cities with populations between 5,000-50,000 must have sewerage (or
water) tariffs equal to or higher than 60% of the long-run average incre-
mental cost. This criteria will be considered equivalent to an IFR of 1% or
higher (i.e. average tariff equal or higher than the marginal cost dis-
counted at a 1% interest rate).
1/ The average tariff used is obtained by dividing the revenues from water
(or sewerage) services by the respective volume billed in each city. When
the present tariff does not satisfy the selection criteria the average
tariff (in constant prices) to be implemented within the following 12
months would be used for a second analysis.



-119-
ANNEX 10
Page 2 of 2
5.         Subprojects to be carried out in communities with populations of
less than 5,000, must have a per capita construction cost 1/ of less than
US$180 in 1980 prices.
6.         Subprojects not meeting the respective criteria must be rede-
signed to lower acceptable standards or carried out in stages.   If the
subproject does not fulfill the criterion after it has been redesigned, it
would be eligible for Bank financing only if a satisfactory social and
economic justification is furnished to the Bank. The social and economic
justification will be made on a case-by-case basis, and will consider among
other things, the closeness to the acceptance limits, the percentage of
urban poor served, the impossibility of providing water by other means or
having additional revenues, the health conditions of the population, and
the evaluation of benefits on other sectors of the economy.
1/   Construction cost divided by the population to be served after the
project construction is completed.

BRAZIL
MULTISATE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
ECONOMIC AND SOCIAL ANALYSIS
Internal Financial Rate of Return (IFR) and Marginal Cost
Calculations
1.01      Internal Financial rates of return (IFR) have been calculated
for the water component and for the water supply and sewerage component on
the capital cities in each state (Tables 1 to 6), since sewerage charges
are related to the volume of water used and it is not possible for con-
sumers to indicate their willingness to pay for each service independ-
ently. A separate calculation for water supply is provided in view of the
fact that a large majority of consumers is not sewered. All investments
have been stated in January 1981 prices in ORTN and include engineering and
physical contingencies. Foreign exchange has been shadow priced at 1.15
times the official rate of exchange. The labor shadow price was set at 0.9
times the average wage.   Project investments, as well as complementary
investments to be made by the SWCs during and after project execution have
been included in the analysis; small investments not related to the incre-
mental benefits have been excluded (such as studies for future projects,
pipe or equipment replacement).   Water being provided without treatment
will be replaced by treated water in the cities of Manaus (0.4 m3/second),
Belem (0.6 m3/per second) and Brasilia (1.2 m3/second by 1984). The cost
and benefit of providing safe water has been estimated on the basis of 50%
of such volumes, starting in 1984. The project benefits have been extended
until the production capacity is used by 90% (to consider daily maximum
demand). Complementary investments are mainly house connections (about 4
ORTN each) which would be largely financed by urban developers, contribu-
tions to construction (BNH urban poverty housing programs) and some con-
nection fees.
1.02      The SWCs, with the exception of CAESB are responsible for sev-
eral dozens of cities or towns. Cost accounting is still incipient and the
direct cost values accounted for each city are only a fraction of total
cost. A large proportion of the personnel and service costs is considered
administration.   The use of the direct costs would have underestimated
operating expenses.   The operating expenses for the capital cities were
therefore estimated on the basis of total costs prorated by the number of
connections.l/  Expenses for personnel, chemicals, power, materials and
services for water and sewerage, as well as general administrative costs
were projected on the basis of house connections or volume produced. De-
tails are available in project file.
1/ Unit costs may be larger due to higher volumes per connection in the
capitals, but this is compensated by economies of scale.



- 121 -
ANNEX 11
Page 2 of 14
1.03      Revenues generated by the project have been used as a proxy for
economic benefits.  The average tariffs for August 1980, as well as the
covenanted tariffs (level of tariffs required by the financial covenant)
during project execution were expressed in prices of January 1981.  Al-
though the same tariff structure is used for all cities within a SWC, the
average tariff is always higher in the capital cities due to the effect of
industrial and commercial users, and a higher proportion of large residen-
tial users which purchase water at excess consumption charges 2 to 2.5
higher than for minimum consumption. The tariffs in ORTN/thousand m3 used
in the analysis,  (in January 1981 prices)  are showed below.   Sewerage
benefits have been estimated at present (80-100%) levels of charges.
COSAMA    COSANPA   CAGEPA    SANEAGO   SANESUL   CAESB
Present tariff
capital             31.60      16.70      22.73     19.40      16.31      9.53

a/  Highest tariff, in January 1981 prices, required by the Bank covenant
and Brazilian Tariff Law between 1981-84.
1.04      The IFR was also estimated for the consolidated water and sewer-
age investments at present and covenanted tariff (details in pages 4 to 9
of this annex). The results and weighted averages are shown in table 8.2.
1.05      The marginal cost for the investments on the capital cities have
been estimated as the average long term incremental cost for project and
complementary investments. The incremental values are calculated on pages
4 to 9 of this Annex and are summarized in Table 8.1.
1.06      The analysis of the marginal cost and IFR have been estimated
separately for the total water and water and sewerage investments of the
SWCs (pages 12 to 16 of this annex). The results should only be considered
a rough estimate since the total investment includes many subprojects in
different degrees of preparation, for which the precise analysis of costs
and benefits is not feasible. Furthermore, for the consolidated results is
difficult to eliminate costs unrelated to the incremental benefits (fixed
assets replacement) or establish the year for which subprojects capacity
would be reached.
1.07      The justification of each individual subproject will be made
using the average long-run incremental cost (marginal cost) calculated on
the basis of the investment program of the project and its complementary
works. Projects in which the present tariff or that to be implemented in
the near future exceed 75% 1/ of the marginal cost (equivalent to an IFR of
1/   60% for sewerage or water supply in cities with less than 50,000
inhabitants (equivalent to about 1% IFR).



- 122 -
ANNEX 11
Page 3 of 14
5% or more) would be approved. More expensive projects will be reviewed to
standards affordable to the population. The use of marginal cost simpli-
fies the project selection (specially under large tariff variations), pro-
vide an useful insights of costs structures, and is easier to understand by
policy makers. The marginal cost will give very useful statistics on per
capita investment costs, operating expenses and the cities were tariff
surcharges and intensive metering should be applied.
Economic Rate of Return and IFR
1.08      The IFR at present tariff measures only a part of the project
benefits. The results of the IFR can get closer to the economic return of
the project if the consumer surplus is included. The consumer surplus can
be estimated if the price-elasticity is known.  Two points on the demand
curve are known:  the average charges (YI) and consumption (X ), and the
price paid by urban poor to water vendors (Y ) for very bmall amounts of
water (X ).  An exponential function of the form Y = C e  x can be fitted
between these points.   The ratio  (k) between  the social and economic
benefits perceived by the users and the IFR will be the area below this
function divided by the benefits estimated by the traditional IFR (X1
multiplied by Y1).  The integration of Y = cebx between X  and X1 (details
in project file) provides the area A = (X  - X IXY  - YI)/ln (Y /Y0).  The
project benefits, including consumer surplus, are then k times higher than
those measured by the IFR.
k = (Y- Y 0)/(Y1 ln (Y1/Y0))  (l  Y0/Y1)/In(Y 1/Y0
1.09      Urban poor purchase small amounts of water (20 liters per capita
per day or about 2-3 m3 per family per month) at prices between 4 and 6
times the average price for piped water. For a ratio of Y to Y of 4, k =
2.20, and the consumer surplus can be estimated as the 9FR at a tariff
equal to 2.2 times the average tariff. The rates of return, including the
estimated consumer surplus are included in Table 8.2.
Urban poverty
1.10      The levels of urban poverty, defined as families with an income
lower than three minimum salaries were estimated by the appraisal team
(Table 8.3) using the scarce information available and by visits to the
capital cities. Census data for 1970 are considered obsolete; a new census
was started in September 1980 but its detailed results (by cities) will not
be available for this appraisal report. The number of urban poor presently
served is generally low because the overall levels of services are low (for
water supply 67% on the capitals and 60% on the SWCs) and persons with
higher income usually receive piped water first.  The analysis of incre-
mental population is detailed in pages 4 to 9 of this annex, and the impact
on poverty in the capital cities is summarized in Table 8.3.
1/ "Estudo nacional de despesas familiares" - ENDEF 8/1975; "Pesquisa
nacional por amostra da Domicilios" IBGE 1978.
